Two Types of Naqd in Jadal
Author explains the second method of response: naqd with two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of naqd (lack of entailment and entailment of impossibility) and apply them to evaluate an opponent's proof in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Naqd attacks the whole proof by showing either it doesn't prove the claim or it leads to absurdity.

In jadal, when the mu'allil's proof is objected to, one powerful response is naqd — a refutation of the opponent's own proof. This lesson explores the two ways to carry out naqd as explained by the author, using concrete examples from geometry and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles: mu'allil and mu'arid
The mu'allil is the one defending a claim; the mu'arid is the opponent objecting to the proof. In naqd, the mu'allil attacks the mu'arid's proof.
2
Recognize the first type: lack of entailment
The mu'arid's proof exists but does not entail the conclusion. The example: a shape defined as 'a surface surrounded by four lines' applies to rectangles and parallelograms, not only squares, so the proof fails.
This is about the proof not being sufficient to prove the specific claim.
3
Recognize the second type: entailment of impossibility
The mu'arid's proof leads to an absurdity like circularity or infinite regress. The example: the atheist's proof that the universe originated by accident implies the existence depends on itself (circularity).
This shows the proof is logically impossible.
4
Practice identifying type
Given a new argument, determine which type of naqd applies. For instance, 'This act is obligatory because it is commanded.' Could you use lack of entailment? (Maybe not all commanded acts are obligatory.)
Think of counterexamples or absurd consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعلوم أن مقدمات الأدلة لا تخرج عن كونها دعاوى في قضايا. وقد عرفنا أيضاً أن البديهي الجلي لا يصح توجيه المنع إليه، بل يجب التسليم به . الوجه الثاني : أن يجيب بالنقض ويسمى (النقض الإجمالي)(١). وذلك بأن يثبت المعلل فساد دليل السائل (المعارض). وفساده يكون بأحد أمرين : أ ـ إما بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، فيظهر بذلك أن الدليل غير صالح للاستدلال به. مثل: (هذا الشكل مربع، لأنه سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط) فيأتي النقض بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل في المستطيل ومتوازي الأضلاع، فكل منهما ينطبق عليه أنه سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط . ب ـ وإما باستلزام الدليل المحال، كالدور السبقي، والتسلسل. مثل قول الملحد: (هذا الكون لا خالق له وإن كان حادثاً، لأنه وجد بنفسه مصادفة). فيأتي النقض بأن هذا الدليل يستلزم المحال وهو الدور، إذ يستلزم توقف وجود الشيء على وجود نفسه . وسيأتي شرح النقض في مبحث خاص، مع بيان أقسامه . الوجه الثالث : أن يثبت المعلل دعواه بدليل آخر غير الدليل الذي أورد السائل عليه المعارضة . ويرى البعض عدم جواز هذا، لأنه انتقال من حجة إلى حجة أخرى، بعد إبطال الحجة الأولى، فلا يفيد المعلل أن يلجأ إليه . ويرى آخرون أن ذلك يفيده، لاحتمال أن يكون الدليل الآخر فيه تدعيم وتقوية للدليل الأول، فهما باجتماعهما يقويان على المعارضة التي أوردها السائل. وهذا هو المختار.
(۱) يسمى (النقض الإجمالي) لأن موجهه لا يتعرض لمقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل بالإبطال، بل يوجهه إلى الدليل جملة واحدة .
And it is known that the premises of proofs do not go beyond being claims in propositions. And we have also known that the clear self-evident cannot be directed with prevention, rather it must be accepted. The second way: that he responds with refutation (naqd) and it is called (the summary refutation). That is by the mu'allil establishing the corruption of the proof of the questioner (the opponent). And its corruption is in one of two matters: A) Either by the lack of entailment of the implied by the proof, so it appears that the proof is not valid for inference. For example: (This shape is a square, because it is a surface surrounded by four lines) so the refutation comes by the lack of entailment of the implied by the proof in the rectangle and parallelogram, for each of them is applicable to it that it is a surface surrounded by four lines. B) Or by the proof entailing an impossibility, like prior circularity and infinite regress. For example, the statement of the atheist: (This universe has no creator even if it is originated, because it came into existence by itself accidentally). So the refutation comes that this proof entails an impossibility which is circularity, for it entails the dependence of the existence of a thing on its own existence. And the explanation of naqd will come in a special section, with a statement of its divisions. The third way: that the mu'allil establishes his claim by another proof other than the proof against which the opponent brought the objection. And some see that this is not permissible, because it is a transition from one proof to another proof after the first proof has been invalidated, so it does not benefit the mu'allil to resort to it. And others see that it benefits him, due to the possibility that the other proof may contain strengthening and reinforcement of the first proof, so together they strengthen against the objection brought by the questioner. And this is the preferred view. (Footnote: It is called summary refutation because its director does not target a particular premise of the premises of the proof with invalidation, but rather directs it to the proof as a whole.)
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage discusses the admissible responses of a mu'allil (defender of a claim) when faced with a mu'arida (objection) from a questioner in jadal (debate). The author first reminds that all premises of proofs are claims in propositions, and that clear self-evident propositions cannot be challenged but must be accepted. Then he presents the second way of response: naqd or refutation, also called summary refutation (al-naqd al-ijmali). The mu'allil demonstrates the invalidity of the questioner's proof by either showing that the conclusion does not follow from the proof (the proof is present but the conclusion is not), using an example of a shape he calls a square but the definition applies to other shapes too; or by showing that the proof entails an impossibility like circularity or infinite regress, giving the example of an atheist saying the universe came into existence by accident without a creator. The author notes that a detailed explanation of naqd will come later. Then he presents the third way: the mu'allil produces a completely different proof for his claim instead of the one that was objected to. There is a dispute about this: some say it is not allowed because it is shifting from one proof to another after the first has been invalidated, so it does not benefit. Others say it does benefit because the second proof may strengthen the first, and together they become stronger against the objection. The author chooses this latter view. The footnote clarifies that the 'summary refutation' does not target a specific premise but the proof as a whole.

المعلل
المعارض
الدور السبقي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two ways to demonstrate the corruption of the opponent's proof in naqd?
A) By showing the proof is weak or that it contradicts the opponent's own statements.
B) By showing the conclusion does not follow (lack of entailment) or that the proof entails an impossibility.
C) By showing the proof is not accepted by scholars or that it is based on weak premises.
D) By showing the proof is ambiguous or that it requires further clarification.

Key Takeaway
Naqd is a refutation that attacks the opponent's proof as a whole, either by showing it fails to entail the conclusion or by showing it leads to an impossibility.

?

The Third Way: Bringing a New Proof After Objection
Author presents the third method of response and the dispute over its permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the arguments for and against the permissibility of bringing a new proof after the first is invalidated, based on the passage.

Core Idea
Bringing a new proof after invalidation is controversial, but the author permits it for strengthening the original argument.

After an objection is raised, the mu'allil has a third option: produce an entirely different proof for his claim. This lesson examines the disagreement among scholars about this practice and the author's preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the third way position
The mu'allil can establish his claim with a different proof, not the one that was objected to.
2
Understand the first objection
Some scholars say this is not permissible because it is 'transition from one proof to another after invalidating the first' and thus does not benefit the mu'allil.
They see it as a sign of weakness.
3
Understand the second view
Other scholars argue it is permissible because the new proof may strengthen and reinforce the first proof, making them together stronger against the objection.
This is the view the author sides with.
4
Identify the author's preferred view
The author says 'this is the preferred view' (al-mukhtar), meaning he favors the permissibility of bringing a new proof.
5
Apply reasoning to a scenario
Consider a debate about whether a certain financial transaction is riba. If the first proof is objected to, would bringing a new proof be beneficial? Discuss the pros and cons.
Use the arguments from the passage.

Naqd in Dialectical Debate: Types and Conditions
Author defines naqd, its two types, condition of requiring a shahid, and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of naqd and the condition requiring a shahid in dialectical arguments.

Core Idea
Naqd is a structured refutation that must be supported by a shahid unless self-evident.

In Islamic dialectical debate (jadal), refuting an opponent's proof is not merely stating it is wrong. The method of naqd provides a rigorous way to challenge a proof by either showing the conclusion does not follow or that the proof leads to an absurdity. This lesson unpacks the definition, types, and the crucial requirement of providing evidence (shahid) for the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of naqd
Read the author's definition: naqd is the questioner's claim that the mu'allil's proof is invalid, accompanied by his own argument for invalidity.
This is not a simple denial; it requires a counter-argument.
2
Distinguish the two types of naqd
Find the two ways to invalidate a proof: (1) takhalluf al-madlul 'an al-dalil (the conclusion does not follow), and (2) istilzam al-muhal (the proof entails an impossibility like circularity or infinite regress).
These correspond to showing the proof is not valid or that it is absurd.
3
Understand the shahid condition and its exception
Note that naqd is only accepted if accompanied by a shahid (proof that demonstrates the corruption of the mu'allil's proof), except when the naqd is self-evident (badihi), in which case its self-evidence suffices.
The shahid is the evidence for the refutation, not just a witness.
4
Recognize the compound syllogism structure
The footnote explains that naqd is equivalent to a compound syllogism with a suppressed premise, e.g., 'This proof lacks entailment; every such proof is invalid; therefore it is invalid.'
This shows the logical backbone of a valid naqd.

الطريق الثالثة (النقض): ومن طرق المناظرة حول التصديق النظري المقرون بالدليل عليه، والتصديق البديهي المقرون بالتنبيه، طريق النقض. والمراد من النقض ادعاء السائل بطلان دليل المعلل، مع استدلاله على دعوى البطلان. إما بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، بمعنى أن يكون الدليل موجوداً والمدلول ليس بموجود، إذ يكون الدليل جارياً على مدعى آخر غير هذا المدعى. وإما بسبب استلزامه المحال، أو نحو ذلك(١). ولا يقبل النقض إلا مقترناً بشاهد، باستثناء حالة واحدة هي أن يكون النقض بديهياً، وحينئذ تقوم بداهته مقام الشاهد. تعريف الشاهد: والمراد بالشاهد هنا: ما يدلُّ على فساد دليل المعلل. أو بعبارة أخرى: الدليل الذي يدل على صحة النقض، فيبين هذا الشاهد وجود دليل المعلل مع عدم وجود مدعاه، فيظهر بذلك تخلف المدلول عن الدليل. أو يبين هذا الشاهد فساد دليل المعلل لأنه يستلزم الأمر الفلاني وهو محال، بسبب كونه من قبيل الدور السبقي مثلاً، أو بسبب أن فيه تسلسلاً، أو نحو ذلك. أمثلة للنقض: أ ـ المعلل (على مذهب الفلاسفة): هذا الكون قديم (مدعى)، لأنه أثر للقديم، وكل ما هو أثر للقديم فهو قديم.
(۱) الاعتراض بالنقض في قوة دليل مركب بحذف بعض مقدماته، وصورته أن يقول مثلاً: هذا الدليل تخلف عنه مدلوله، وكل دليل كان كذلك فهو فاسد. أو يقول: هذا الدليل مستلزم للمحال، وكل دليل كذلك فهو فاسد.
The third way: Refutation (naqd). Among the methods of debate concerning theoretical assent accompanied by evidence upon it, and self-evident assent accompanied by awareness, is the method of refutation. What is meant by refutation is the claim of the questioner of the invalidity of the proof of the mu'allil, along with his inference for the claim of invalidity. Either by the lack of entailment of the implied by the proof, meaning that the proof exists but the implied does not exist, so the proof applies to another claim other than this claim. Or because of its entailing an impossibility, or the like. And refutation is not accepted unless accompanied by a shahid, with one exception: that the refutation be self-evident, in which case its self-evidence stands in place of the shahid. Definition of shahid: What is meant by shahid here is that which indicates the corruption of the proof of the mu'allil. Or in other words, the proof that indicates the correctness of the refutation, so this shahid shows the existence of the proof of the mu'allil without the existence of his claim, thus showing the lack of entailment of the implied by the proof. Or this shahid shows the corruption of the proof of the mu'allil because it entails such-and-such impossible, due to being of the type of prior circularity, for example, or because it involves infinite regress, or the like. Examples of refutation: A) The mu'allil (according to the philosophers): This universe is eternal (claim), because it is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal. (Footnote: The objection by refutation is equivalent to a compound syllogism by deleting some of its premises. Its form is that he says, for example: This proof is not entailed by its implied, and every proof like that is invalid. Or he says: This proof entails an impossibility, and every proof like that is invalid.)
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of naqd as the third way of debate concerning both theoretical assent that is accompanied by evidence and self-evident assent that is accompanied by awareness. The author defines naqd as the claim of the questioner that the mu'allil's proof is invalid, along with his own argument for why it is invalid. This can be done in two ways: First, by showing that the conclusion does not follow from the proof, meaning the proof is present but the claimed conclusion is absent, indicating that the proof actually supports a different claim. Second, by showing that the proof entails an impossibility, such as circular reasoning or infinite regress. The author stipulates that naqd is only accepted if it is accompanied by a shahid (witness or proof), unless the naqd itself is self-evident, in which case its self-evident nature suffices. He then defines shahid as whatever indicates the corruption of the mu'allil's proof, either by showing the existence of the proof without the conclusion, or by showing that the proof leads to an absurdity. Finally, he gives an example: the mu'allil (on the philosophers' view) claims the universe is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal. The footnote clarifies that an objection by naqd is equivalent to a compound syllogism where some premises are suppressed, having the form: 'This proof lacks entailment of its conclusion, and every proof lacking entailment is invalid' or 'This proof entails an impossibility, and every proof entailing impossibility is invalid.'

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): The claim that the opponent's proof is invalid, substantiated by showing either lack of entailment or entailing impossibility. [Note: Standard in jadal; also used in other sciences to mean 'refutation'.]
الشاهد (al-shahid): The proof that demonstrates the corruption of the mu'allil's proof, either by showing lack of entailment or by showing it leads to impossibility. [Note: In legal contexts, shahid means witness; here it is a logical proof.]
تخلف المدلول عن الدليل (takhalluf al-madlul 'an al-dalil): The situation where the proof exists but the claimed conclusion does not, indicating the proof is not valid for that conclusion. [Note: A key concept in jadal for evaluating proofs.]
استلزام المحال (istilzam al-muhal): The proof entailing an impossibility, such as circularity or infinite regress. [Note: Standard in logical critiques of arguments.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the exception to the rule that naqd must be accompanied by a shahid?
A) When the naqd is presented by a respected scholar.
B) When the naqd is self-evident.
C) When the mu'allil agrees with the naqd.
D) When the naqd is based on a widely known fact.

Key Takeaway
Naqd in jadal is not a mere denial but a structured refutation that requires evidential support unless self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Using the example from the passage, construct a naqd against the following proof: 'The Quran is created (makhluq) because it is contingent (mumkin).' Identify whether you are using takhalluf or istilzam al-muhal and propose a shahid.
?
Task 2: Evaluate a given naqd in a debate context: if the naqd states 'Your proof that the world has a Creator because it is designed entails an impossibility because design requires a prior design...', determine if this naqd requires a shahid or if it is self-evident.

The Definition and Structure of Naqd (Refutation)
Defines naqd, its condition, and its syllogistic structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define naqd, distinguish its two types, and explain the requirement of a witness.

Core Idea
Naqd is a structured objection requiring proof that the arguer's evidence fails or leads to impossibility.

In the tradition of Islamicate dialectic (munazara), not every challenge to a proof is legitimate. The method of naqd (refutation) provides a rigorous framework for objecting: the questioner must not only claim the proof is invalid but also provide evidence for that claim. This lesson introduces the definition of naqd, its types, and the crucial role of the shahid (witness).

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of naqd
Find where the author states 'wal-murad min al-naqd...' and note the two ways a naqd can be made: takhalluf al-madlul or istilzam al-muhal.
The Arabic terms are given in the passage.
2
Understand the condition of the shahid
The passage states that naqd is not accepted without a shahid, except when the naqd is intuitive. Reflect on why the shahid is needed: to prove the invalidity claim.
3
Reconstruct the implied syllogism
The author says the objection by naqd has the force of a compound syllogism. Write the syllogism in standard form: Major premise: Every evidence that fails to produce its conclusion (or leads to impossibility) is invalid. Minor premise: This evidence fails... Conclusion: This evidence is invalid.
This exposes the logical backbone of naqd.
4
Apply the concept
Given a simple theological argument, formulate a naqd by identifying which of the two types applies and what the witness would be.
Practice ensures understanding.

دعوى البطلان.
إما بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، بمعنى أن يكون الدليل موجوداً
والمدلول ليس بموجود، إذ يكون الدليل جارياً على مدعى آخر غير هذا
المدعى.

ولا يقبل النقض إلا مقترناً بشاهد، باستثناء حالة واحدة هي أن يكون
النقض بديهياً، وحينئذ تقوم بداهته مقام الشاهد.

تفسير الاعتراض بالنقض: الاعتراض بالنقض في قوة دليل مركب بحذف بعض مقدماته، وصورته أن يقول مثلاً: هذا الدليل تخلف عنه مدلوله، وكل دليل كان كذلك فهو فاسد. أو يقول: هذا الدليل مستلزم للمحال، وكل دليل كذلك فهو فاسد.

تعريف الشاهد:
والمراد بالشاهد هنا: ما يدلُّ على فساد دليل المعلل. أو بعبارة أخرى:
الدليل الذي يدل على صحة النقض، فيبين هذا الشاهد وجود دليل المعلل
مع عدم وجود مدعاه، فيظهر بذلك تخلف المدلول عن الدليل. أو يبين هذا
الشاهد فساد دليل المعلل لأنه يستلزم الأمر الفلاني وهو محال، بسبب كونه
من قبيل الدور السبقي مثلاً، أو بسبب أن فيه تسلسلاً، أو نحو ذلك.

أمثلة للنقض:
أ ـ المعلل (على مذهب الفلاسفة): هذا الكون قديم (مدعى)، لأنه
أثر للقديم، وكل ما هو أثر للقديم فهو قديم.
السائل: هذا الدليل باطل منقوض، لأنه ينطبق على الحوادث اليومية التي نشاهدها باستمرار، فهي أيضاً أثر للقديم، فلو صح الدليل في إثباته المدّعى للزم أن تكون الحوادث اليومية قديمة، لكونها أثراً للقديم أيضاً، مع أن كونها حادثة أمر بدهي.

ب - المعلل: الحدُّ له تعريف (مدّعى).
لأنه تعريف، وكل تعريف له تعريف.
السائل: هذا الدليل باطل منقوض، لأنه يستلزم المحال، وهو التسلسل إلى غير نهاية، إذ المقدمة الكبرى في الدليل (وكل تعريف له تعريف) تقتضي أنه كلما جيء بتعريف وجب تعريفه بتعريف آخر إلى غير نهاية، وهذا تسلسل محال.

جـ - المعلل الملحد بالله: هذا الكون لا خالق له (مدّعى)، لأنه أوجد نفسه بنفسه، وكل ما أوجد نفسه بنفسه فلا خالق له.
السائل المؤمن: هذا الدليل باطل منقوض، لأنه يستلزم المحال، وهو هنا الدور السبقي، إذ المقدمة الصغرى (الكون أوجد نفسه بنفسه) تستلزم قبول الدور السبقي المحال، وذلك لأن الكون لا يوجد نفسه حتى يكون
The third method (al-naqd): Among the methods of debate concerning theoretical assent accompanied by evidence, and intuitive assent accompanied by prompting, is the method of naqd. And the meaning of naqd is the claim of the questioner of the invalidity of the evidence of the arguer, accompanied by his proof of the claim of invalidity. Either by the inferred conclusion not following from the evidence, meaning that the evidence exists while the inferred conclusion does not exist, as the evidence applies to another claim other than this claim. Or because it entails an impossibility, or the like. And naqd is not accepted unless accompanied by a witness, except in one case: that the naqd is intuitive, in which case its intuitiveness takes the place of the witness. Interpretation of objection by naqd: The objection by naqd has the force of a compound syllogism with some of its premises omitted. Its form is, for example, to say: 'This evidence is not followed by its conclusion, and every evidence that is like that is invalid.' Or to say: 'This evidence entails an impossibility, and every evidence that does so is invalid.' Definition of the witness: What is meant by witness here is that which indicates the unsoundness of the arguer's evidence. Or in other words: the evidence that indicates the validity of the naqd, so this witness shows the existence of the arguer's evidence together with the non-existence of his claim, thereby showing the lack of consequence of the inferred conclusion from the evidence. Or this witness shows the unsoundness of the arguer's evidence because it entails a certain thing which is impossible, due to it being of the type of circular priority, for example, or because it involves an infinite regress, or the like. Examples of naqd: A – The arguer (according to the doctrine of the philosophers): 'This universe is eternal (claim), because it is an effect of the eternal, and everything that is an effect of the eternal is eternal.' The questioner: 'This evidence is invalid, refuted, because it applies to the daily events that we constantly witness, for they are also effects of the eternal. If the evidence were valid in proving the claim, it would necessitate that the daily events be eternal, because they are also effects of the eternal, while their being temporal is intuitive.' B – The arguer: 'A definition has a definition (claim), because it is a definition, and every definition has a definition.' The questioner: 'This evidence is invalid, refuted, because it entails an impossibility, which is infinite regress. For the major premise in the evidence – 'every definition has a definition' – requires that whenever a definition is brought, it must be defined by another definition ad infinitum, and this is an impossible regress.' C – The atheist arguer concerning God: 'This universe has no creator (claim), because it brought itself into existence, and everything that brought itself into existence has no creator.' The believing questioner: 'This evidence is invalid, refuted, because it entails an impossibility, which is circular priority. The minor premise – 'the universe brought itself into existence' – entails accepting impossible circular priority, because the universe does not bring itself into existence until it is...'
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the method of naqd, a key dialectical tool in Islamicate logic and debate. The author opens by stating that naqd is the third method (after others presumably discussed earlier) used in dispute (munazara) regarding theoretical assent supported by evidence and intuitive assent requiring prompting. The term 'naqd' literally means 'refutation' or 'objection,' but here it is given a technical meaning: the questioner's claim that the arguer's proof is invalid, and the questioner must also provide evidence for that claim of invalidity. There are two ways to make a naqd: either by showing that the claimed conclusion does not actually follow from the evidence (the evidence is present but the conclusion is absent, meaning the evidence actually supports a different claim), or by showing that the evidence leads to an absurdity (muhal) such as circularity or infinite regress. The author then gives a detailed 'interpretation' (tafsir) of the objection by naqd, noting that it effectively amounts to a compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) with some premises omitted. Its standard form would be: 'This evidence fails to produce its conclusion (or leads to absurdity), and every evidence that does so is invalid.' The naqd is only accepted if it is accompanied by a 'witness' (shahid), which is evidence that demonstrates the unsoundness of the original proof. The only exception is when the naqd itself is intuitively obvious (badihi), in which case its obviousness substitutes for the witness. The shahid is defined as that which indicates the corruption of the arguer's evidence. It works either by showing that the evidence exists while the claimed conclusion does not (thus separating evidential support from the claim) or by showing that the evidence entails an impossibility like circular priority (al-dawr al-sabqi) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author then gives three concrete examples, each drawn from theological or philosophical debates. The first example involves a philosopher arguing that the universe is eternal because it is an effect of the eternal. The questioner refutes this by pointing out that the same evidence would apply to everyday events, which are also effects of the eternal, yet they are clearly temporal (haditha). Thus the evidence fails to prove the claim (it would prove too much). The second example involves an arguer claiming that 'a definition has a definition' because it is a definition. The questioner points out that this leads to an infinite regress of definitions, which is logically impossible. The third example is an atheist claiming the universe has no creator because it brought itself into existence. The believing questioner responds that this leads to circular priority (al-dawr al-sabqi): the universe cannot bring itself into existence before it exists. The third example is incomplete in the OCR, but the pattern is clear. Overall, this passage provides a thorough methodological treatment of naqd, showing its theoretical basis and practical application in dialectical exchanges.

Key Terms
النقض
الشاهد
المدلول
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Naqd is a dialectical objection structured as a categorical syllogism, requiring a witness unless the objection is self-evident.

Applying Naqd: The Role of the Witness and Analyzing Examples
Defines the shahid and provides three examples of naqd from theological and philosophical debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the shahid in a naqd, and analyze a dialectical exchange to determine how the naqd invalidates the argument.

Core Idea
The witness is the crucial evidence that proves the arguer's proof is unsound.

In the previous lesson, we learned the definition and structure of naqd. Now we turn to the practical application: the witness (shahid) and the famous examples from theology and philosophy. These examples concretely show how naqd works as a method of refutation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of shahid
The passage defines the shahid as 'that which indicates the unsoundness of the arguer's evidence'. Understand its two functions: showing the conclusion does not follow, or showing the evidence entails impossibility.
2
Analyze the first example
Read the example of the philosopher arguing for the eternity of the universe. Identify the arguer's claim, evidence, and the questioner's naqd.
3
Extract the witness
In the questioner's response, find the part that serves as the witness. Here, the witness is the observation that daily events also fit the evidence but are not eternal.
4
Determine the type of naqd
Is it a case of takhalluf al-madlul or istilzam al-muhal? In this example, it is takhalluf: the evidence (being an effect of the eternal) exists for daily events, but the conclusion (eternality) does not apply to them.
5
Evaluate the validity
The naqd successfully shows that the evidence fails to prove the claim, because if it did, it would prove too much.

The third method (al-naqd): Among the methods of debate concerning theoretical assent accompanied by evidence, and intuitive assent accompanied by prompting, is the method of naqd. And the meaning of naqd is the claim of the questioner of the invalidity of the evidence of the arguer, accompanied by his proof of the claim of invalidity. Either by the inferred conclusion not following from the evidence, meaning that the evidence exists while the inferred conclusion does not exist, as the evidence applies to another claim other than this claim. Or because it entails an impossibility, or the like. And naqd is not accepted unless accompanied by a witness, except in one case: that the naqd is intuitive, in which case its intuitiveness takes the place of the witness. Interpretation of objection by naqd: The objection by naqd has the force of a compound syllogism with some of its premises omitted. Its form is, for example, to say: 'This evidence is not followed by its conclusion, and every evidence that is like that is invalid.' Or to say: 'This evidence entails an impossibility, and every evidence that does so is invalid.' Definition of the witness: What is meant by witness here is that which indicates the unsoundness of the arguer's evidence. Or in other words: the evidence that indicates the validity of the naqd, so this witness shows the existence of the arguer's evidence together with the non-existence of his claim, thereby showing the lack of consequence of the inferred conclusion from the evidence. Or this witness shows the unsoundness of the arguer's evidence because it entails a certain thing which is impossible, due to it being of the type of circular priority, for example, or because it involves an infinite regress, or the like. Examples of naqd: A – The arguer (according to the doctrine of the philosophers): 'This universe is eternal (claim), because it is an effect of the eternal, and everything that is an effect of the eternal is eternal.' The questioner: 'This evidence is invalid, refuted, because it applies to the daily events that we constantly witness, for they are also effects of the eternal. If the evidence were valid in proving the claim, it would necessitate that the daily events be eternal, because they are also effects of the eternal, while their being temporal is intuitive.' B – The arguer: 'A definition has a definition (claim), because it is a definition, and every definition has a definition.' The questioner: 'This evidence is invalid, refuted, because it entails an impossibility, which is infinite regress. For the major premise in the evidence – 'every definition has a definition' – requires that whenever a definition is brought, it must be defined by another definition ad infinitum, and this is an impossible regress.' C – The atheist arguer concerning God: 'This universe has no creator (claim), because it brought itself into existence, and everything that brought itself into existence has no creator.' The believing questioner: 'This evidence is invalid, refuted, because it entails an impossibility, which is circular priority. The minor premise – 'the universe brought itself into existence' – entails accepting impossible circular priority, because the universe does not bring itself into existence until it is...'
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the method of naqd, a key dialectical tool in Islamicate logic and debate. The author opens by stating that naqd is the third method (after others presumably discussed earlier) used in dispute (munazara) regarding theoretical assent supported by evidence and intuitive assent requiring prompting. The term 'naqd' literally means 'refutation' or 'objection,' but here it is given a technical meaning: the questioner's claim that the arguer's proof is invalid, and the questioner must also provide evidence for that claim of invalidity. There are two ways to make a naqd: either by showing that the claimed conclusion does not actually follow from the evidence (the evidence is present but the conclusion is absent, meaning the evidence actually supports a different claim), or by showing that the evidence leads to an absurdity (muhal) such as circularity or infinite regress. The author then gives a detailed 'interpretation' (tafsir) of the objection by naqd, noting that it effectively amounts to a compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) with some premises omitted. Its standard form would be: 'This evidence fails to produce its conclusion (or leads to absurdity), and every evidence that does so is invalid.' The naqd is only accepted if it is accompanied by a 'witness' (shahid), which is evidence that demonstrates the unsoundness of the original proof. The only exception is when the naqd itself is intuitively obvious (badihi), in which case its obviousness substitutes for the witness. The shahid is defined as that which indicates the corruption of the arguer's evidence. It works either by showing that the evidence exists while the claimed conclusion does not (thus separating evidential support from the claim) or by showing that the evidence entails an impossibility like circular priority (al-dawr al-sabqi) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author then gives three concrete examples, each drawn from theological or philosophical debates. The first example involves a philosopher arguing that the universe is eternal because it is an effect of the eternal. The questioner refutes this by pointing out that the same evidence would apply to everyday events, which are also effects of the eternal, yet they are clearly temporal (haditha). Thus the evidence fails to prove the claim (it would prove too much). The second example involves an arguer claiming that 'a definition has a definition' because it is a definition. The questioner points out that this leads to an infinite regress of definitions, which is logically impossible. The third example is an atheist claiming the universe has no creator because it brought itself into existence. The believing questioner responds that this leads to circular priority (al-dawr al-sabqi): the universe cannot bring itself into existence before it exists. The third example is incomplete in the OCR, but the pattern is clear. Overall, this passage provides a thorough methodological treatment of naqd, showing its theoretical basis and practical application in dialectical exchanges.

Key Terms
الشاهد
النقض
المحال
الدور السبقي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A successful naqd requires a witness that either shows the evidence fails to prove the claim or that it leads to an impossibility.

Refutation by Counterexample: Daily Events Shahid
First example of naqd with shahid from daily events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a naqd by counterexample and use it to refute a proof that produces a false conclusion.

Core Idea
A single counterexample can demolish a supposedly universal proof.

In dialectical debates, the questioner (sa'il) refutes the reasoner's proof by producing a shahid (witness) that shows the proof leads to a false conclusion. In this first example, the proof that 'everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal' is applied to daily events, which are obviously not eternal, exposing the proof's corruption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reasoner's claim and proof
The reasoner claims that the world is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal. The proof is: 'Everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal; the world is an effect of the Eternal; therefore the world is eternal.'
Note that the reasoner's identity is not specified; it could be a theist arguing for eternity of the world.
2
Find the shahid (counterexample)
The questioner points to daily events (al-hawadith al-yawmiyya) that are also effects of the Eternal (as part of creation), but they are obviously originated (haditha), not eternal.
The shahid is a specific instance that fits the proof's premise but contradicts its conclusion.
3
Apply the proof to the shahid
If the proof were valid, it would force the conclusion that daily events are eternal, which is self-evidently false. Therefore, the proof is corrupt (batil) because it yields an absurdity.
The absurdity here is a contradiction with observed reality.
4
Understand the type of naqd
This is naqd by counterexample (also called takhalluf al-madlul 'an al-dalil): the proof's claim does not hold for all instances where the premises apply.
This is one of the two main types of naqd; the other involves logical absurdities like infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السائل: هذا الدليل باطل منقوض، لأنه ينطبق على الحوادث اليومية التي نشاهدها باستمرار، فهي أيضاً أثر للقديم، فلو صح الدليل في إثباته المدّعى للزم أن تكون الحوادث اليومية قديمة، لكونها أثراً للقديم أيضاً، مع أن كونها حادثة أمر بدهي. (شاهد ثبت فيه فساد دليل المعلّل، بوجود هذا الدليل نفسه، مع تخلّف مدّعاه عنه). ب - المعلّل: الحدُّ له تعريف (مدّعى). لأنه تعريف، وكل تعريف له تعريف. (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلل مدعاه) السائل: هذا الدليل باطل منقوض، لأنه يستلزم المحال، وهو التسلسل إلى غير نهاية، إذ المقدمة الكبرى في الدليل (وكل تعريف له تعريف) تقتضي أنه كلما جيء بتعريف وجب تعريفه بتعريف آخر إلى غير نهاية، وهذا تسلسل محال. (شاهد ثبت به فساد دليل المعلّل بأنه يستلزم المحال، وهو هنا التسلسل). جـ - المعلل الملحد بالله: هذا الكون لا خالق له (مدّعى)، لأنه أوجد نفسه بنفسه، وكل ما أوجد نفسه بنفسه فلا خالق له. (دليل فاسد أنتج في نظر الملحد مدّعاه) السائل المؤمن: هذا الدليل باطل منقوض، لأنه يستلزم المحال، وهو هنا الدور السبقي، إذ المقدمة الصغرى (الكون أوجد نفسه بنفسه) تستلزم قبول الدور السبقي المحال، وذلك لأن الكون لا يوجد نفسه حتى يكون موجوداً بالفعل، ولا يكون موجوداً بالفعل حتى يوجد نفسه، وهذا مستحيل بداهة. (شاهد ثبت به فساد دليل المعلّل بسبب أنه يستلزم المحال، وهو هنا الدور السبقي).
The questioner: This proof is false, refuted by naqd, because it applies to the daily events that we constantly observe, for they too are an effect of the Eternal. So if the proof were valid in establishing its claim, it would necessitate that daily events are eternal, because they are an effect of the Eternal as well, despite their being originated being self-evident. (A shahid proving the corruption of the reasoner's proof, by the existence of this same proof, with its claim not following from it). B - The reasoner: The definition has a definition (claim). Because it is a definition, and every definition has a definition. (A proof that produced, in the reasoner's view, his claim). The questioner: This proof is false, refuted by naqd, because it necessitates an absurdity, namely infinite regress. For the major premise in the proof ('every definition has a definition') requires that whenever a definition is brought, it must be defined by another definition ad infinitum, and this is an absurd regress. (A shahid proving the corruption of the reasoner's proof by its necessitating absurdity, which here is regress). C - The atheist reasoner: This universe has no creator (claim), because it brought itself into existence, and everything that brought itself into existence has no creator. (A corrupt proof that produced, in the atheist's view, his claim). The believer questioner: This proof is false, refuted by naqd, because it necessitates an absurdity, which here is circular priority. For the minor premise ('the universe brought itself into existence') entails accepting the impossible circular priority, because the universe cannot bring itself into existence until it actually exists, and it cannot actually exist until it brings itself into existence, and this is self-evidently impossible. (A shahid proving the corruption of the reasoner's proof by its necessitating absurdity, which here is circular priority).
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage provides three concrete examples of naqd in a dialectical exchange. Each example follows a pattern: the reasoner (mu'allil) presents a claim with a proof, and the questioner (sa'il) refutes the proof by producing a shahid (witness/counterexample) that shows the proof is corrupt either because the claim does not follow (takhalluf al-madlul) or because the proof leads to an absurdity (muhal). The first example shows that the proof would also apply to daily events, making them eternal—which is absurd. The second example shows that requiring every definition to be defined leads to infinite regress. The third example involves a cosmological argument from an atheist; the refutation points out circular priority (dawr sababi) in the premise 'the universe brought itself into existence'. These illustrate the two main ways naqd works: either the proof's conclusion is not true for all cases (counterexample) or the proof implies an impossibility.

Naqd by counterexample shows that a proof fails when applied to a specific instance that contradicts its claim.

Refutation by Infinite Regress: Definitions Shahid
Second example of naqd with infinite regress from definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify naqd by infinite regress (tasalsul) and explain why it makes a proof impossible.

Core Idea
If a proof forces an infinite chain of definitions, it collapses into absurdity.

The second example of naqd involves a reasoner who claims that every definition has a definition. The questioner refutes this by showing that it leads to infinite regress (tasalsul): each definition requires another definition ad infinitum, which is impossible. This is a powerful refutation because it does not rely on a specific counterexample but on the logical impossibility of the proof's consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reasoner's claim and proof
The reasoner claims: 'The definition has a definition. Because it is a definition, and every definition has a definition.' This is an attempt to prove that definitions themselves can be defined.
This is a meta-argument about definitions, not about a real-world object.
2
Recognize the absurd implication
The questioner points out that the proof's major premise 'every definition has a definition' implies an infinite regress: to define a definition, you need another definition, and so on forever.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered impossible in classical logic and kalam.
3
Understand why regress is impossible
A regress has no starting point; if no definition is self-defined, then nothing is ever defined. This makes knowledge impossible, which is absurd.
The absurdity here is that the proof contradicts the very possibility of definition.
4
Classify the type of naqd
This is naqd by necessitating absurdity (ilzam bil-muhal). The proof does not require a separate counterexample; its own logical structure leads to impossibility.
This is distinct from the first example where a concrete counterexample was given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation by Circular Priority: Universe Shahid
Third example of naqd with circular priority from self-creation argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify naqd by circular priority (dawr sababi) and explain why it is impossible.

Core Idea
If a thing's existence is presupposed in its own cause, the proof becomes circular and impossible.

The third example involves an atheist who claims the universe created itself. The believer refutes this by showing it entails circular priority (dawr sababi): for the universe to bring itself into existence, it must already exist, creating a vicious circle. This type of naqd exposes internal contradictions in a proof without needing an external counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Identify the atheist's claim and proof
The atheist claims: 'The universe has no creator because it brought itself into existence, and whatever brings itself into existence has no creator.'
This is a common atheistic argument for self-creation.
2
Isolate the problematic premise
The key premise is 'the universe brought itself into existence.' The believer questions this premise by showing it implies circular priority.
Circular priority (dawr sababi) occurs when a thing's existence is required for the very process that brings it into existence.
3
Explain the circularity
For the universe to bring itself into existence, it must already exist in order to perform the act of bringing. But if it already exists, then it did not need to bring itself into existence. This is a vicious circle.
This is impossible (muhal) by rational necessity (badaha).
4
Classify the type of naqd
Again, this is naqd by necessitating absurdity (ilzam bil-muhal). The proof's own premise contradicts itself.
Circular priority is a specific form of absurdity often used in kalam against arguments for self-creation or similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage provides three concrete examples of naqd in a dialectical exchange. Each example follows a pattern: the reasoner (mu'allil) presents a claim with a proof, and the questioner (sa'il) refutes the proof by producing a shahid (witness/counterexample) that shows the proof is corrupt either because the claim does not follow (takhalluf al-madlul) or because the proof leads to an absurdity (muhal). The first example shows that the proof would also apply to daily events, making them eternal—which is absurd. The second example shows that requiring every definition to be defined leads to infinite regress. The third example involves a cosmological argument from an atheist; the refutation points out circular priority (dawr sababi) in the premise 'the universe brought itself into existence'. These illustrate the two main ways naqd works: either the proof's conclusion is not true for all cases (counterexample) or the proof implies an impossibility.

Key Terms
النقض
المحال
الدور السبقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Infinite regress
B) Circular priority
C) Contradiction with observation
D) Self-defeating proof

Key Takeaway
Naqd by circular priority shows that a proof's premise is self-contradictory, making the entire argument unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following argument contains circular priority: 'The Quran is true because it is the word of God, and we know it is the word of God because the Quran says so.' Explain why.
?
Construct a naqd by dawr sababi against the claim: 'The soul is immortal because it can think, and thinking requires an immortal soul.'

Classification of Naqd: Haqiqi and Shabihi
Author divides naqd into two types: haqiqi and shabihi, with further subdivision of haqiqi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given refutation (naqd) into its correct type: haqiqi (real) or shabihi (simulative), and within haqiqi, identify mashhur (famous) or maksur (broken).

Core Idea
The nature of the refutation determines whether you attack the proof itself or the claim's external consistency.

In Islamic dialectical tradition (jadal), not all refutations are the same. The author of this passage divides naqd into two fundamental types: al-naqd al-haqiqi, which targets the proof directly, and al-naqd al-shabihi, which attacks the claim by showing it contradicts an accepted principle. Understanding this classification allows you to choose the most effective rebuttal strategy. In this lesson, you will learn to identify each type and its subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The passage divides naqd into two main types. Find the first type: al-naqd al-haqiqi. Read its definition: it is refutation of the proof without detailing its premises. Note that it is also called al-naqd al-ijmali (total refutation).
2
Examine the subdivision of haqiqi
Under haqiqi, there are two subtypes: al-naqd al-mashhur (famous) and al-naqd al-maksur (broken). For mashhur, the questioner reproduces the entire proof exactly as given, then shows its corruption either by non-sequitur or absurdity.
3
Distinguish haqiqi from shabihi
The second main type is al-naqd al-shabihi. It does not refute the proof but invalidates the claim (da'wa) by pointing to an external inconsistency, such as contradiction with scholarly consensus (ijma') or the reasoner's own doctrine.
4
Apply the classification to a sample
Given a description of a refutation, determine its type. For example, if a questioner says 'Your proof leads to an absurdity because your conclusion contradicts the Qur'an' – is that haqiqi or shabihi? (Answer: shabihi, because it attacks the claim externally.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام النقض: ينقسم النقض إلى نوعين: النوع الأول: (النقض الحقيقي) وهو ردُّ الدليل من غير تفصيل لمقدمتيه. وهذا النوع يسمى أيضاً: (النقض الإجمالي) ومَوْرِدُه دليل المعلّل. وينقسم هذا النوع إلى قسمين: أ ـ النقض المشهور. ب ـ النقض المكسور. النوع الثاني: (النقض الشبيهي) وهو إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فسادٍ مخصوص، كالمخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو المنافاة لمذهب المعلّل، أو نحو ذلك. شرح النقض الحقيقي بقسميه المشهور والمكسور: عرفنا أن النقض الحقيقي معناه ردُّ دليل المعلّل بشاهد يثبت فيه السائل صحة النقض، فمَوْرِد النقض في النقض الحقيقي دليلُ المعلّل. وهو قسمان: نقض مشهور، ونقض مكسور. أما النقض المشهور: فهو النقض الذي يورد فيه السائل كلَّ عناصر دليل المعلّل، فلا يترك منها شيئاً، ويورده على هيئته، فلا يغير فيه شيئاً، ولا يحذف منه شيئاً، ويثبت مع ذلك فساده، بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، أو باستلزام الدليل المحال.
Divisions of naqd: Naqd is divided into two types: The first type: (al-naqd al-haqiqi) which is the refutation of the proof without detailing its premises. This type is also called: (al-naqd al-ijmali) and its target is the reasoner's proof. This type is subdivided into two divisions: A) al-naqd al-mashhur. B) al-naqd al-maksur. The second type: (al-naqd al-shabihi) which is the invalidation of the claim by a specific evidence of corruption, such as contradicting the consensus of scholars, or opposing the school of the reasoner, or the like. Explanation of al-naqd al-haqiqi with its two divisions, the famous and the broken: We have understood that al-naqd al-haqiqi means the refutation of the reasoner's proof with a witness by which the questioner proves the validity of the naqd. So the target of naqd in al-naqd al-haqiqi is the reasoner's proof. It is two divisions: famous naqd and broken naqd. As for al-naqd al-mashhur: it is the naqd in which the questioner brings all the elements of the reasoner's proof, leaving nothing out, and presents it as it is, changing nothing, deleting nothing, and then proves its corruption, either by the non-sequitur of the indicated from the proof, or by the proof entailing absurdity.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage classifies naqd into two main types. First, al-naqd al-haqiqi (real/fundamental refutation) is a direct refutation of the proof itself without dissecting its premises individually; it is also called al-naqd al-ijmali (total refutation). Its target is the reasoner's proof as a whole. This type is further divided into two: (a) al-naqd al-mashhur (the famous/well-known naqd) where the questioner reproduces the entire proof exactly as given, without any alteration, and then demonstrates its corruption either because the conclusion does not follow (takhalluf al-madlul) or because the proof necessitates an absurdity (muhal). (b) al-naqd al-maksur (the broken/obscure naqd) is implied but not detailed in this text; typically it involves a partial or indirect refutation. Second, al-naqd al-shabihi (simulative/quasi-naqd) is not a direct refutation of the proof but an invalidation of the claim by citing an external inconsistency, such as the claim contradicting scholarly consensus (ijma') or the reasoner's own school of thought. This classification helps the dialectician choose the appropriate strategy for rebuttal.

Key Terms

النقض الشبيهي
النقض المشهور
النقض المكسور
الدعوى

C) It invalidates the claim by citing a contradiction with an external principle like consensus.
D) It is a type of naqd that only addresses part of the proof.

Key Takeaway
Naqd is divided into haqiqi (targeting the proof directly) and shabihi (targeting the claim via external inconsistencies), and haqiqi further splits into mashhur (full reproduction) and maksur (partial or indirect).

(Answer: naqd shabihi, because it invalidates the claim by pointing to external consensus, not by refuting the proof.)

Definition and Primary Division of Naqd
Defines naqd and divides it into haqiqi and shibhi with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main types of naqd (refutation) in Islamic dialectic and explain their respective targets.

Core Idea
Naqd is not just any criticism—it has two precise types: attacking the proof (haqiqi) or the claim (shibhi).

In jadal (dialectic), refutation (naqd) is a structured operation. This lesson introduces the primary division of naqd into real refutation (naqd haqiqi), which attacks the prover's proof directly, and semblance refutation (naqd shibhi), which attacks the claim itself. Understanding this distinction is key to following a munazara (debate) correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term defined
The passage defines naqd as the act of invalidating a proof or claim. In jadal, it is a technical term with specific categories.
The Arabic term نَقْض carries the root meaning of breaking or demolishing.
2
Extract the primary division
Naqd is divided into two types: al-naqd al-haqiqi (real refutation) and al-naqd al-shibhi (semblance refutation). Memorize these two categories.
3
Compare the targets
Real refutation targets the prover's proof (dalil al-mu'allil) without analyzing its premises. Semblance refutation targets the claim (da'wa) itself, using evidence of corruption like contradiction with consensus.
The passage explicitly states: 'Mawriduhu dalil al-mu'allil' for haqiqi.
4
Identify subcategories of haqiqi
Real refutation is further divided into famous refutation (naqd mashhur) and broken refutation (naqd maksur). These terms reflect the nature of the flaw in the proof.
The passage does not elaborate on the subcategories, but they are part of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موجوداً بالفعل، ولا يكون موجوداً بالفعل حتى يوجد نفسه، وهذا مستحيل بداهة. (شاهد ثبت به فساد دليل المعلّل بسبب أنه يستلزم المحال، وهو هنا الدور السبقي). أقسام النقض: ينقسم النقض إلى نوعين: النوع الأول: (النقض الحقيقي) وهو ردُّ الدليل من غير تفصيل لمقدمتيه. وهذا النوع يسمى أيضاً: (النقض الإجمالي) ومَوْرِدُه دليل المعلّل. وينقسم هذا النوع إلى قسمين: أ ـ النقض المشهور. ب ـ النقض المكسور. النوع الثاني: (النقض الشبيهي) وهو إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فسادٍ مخصوص، كالمخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو المنافاة لمذهب المعلّل، أو نحو ذلك.
…actually existing, and it does not actually exist until it exists itself, and this is self-evidently impossible. (A witness by which the corruption of the proof of the prover is established, because it entails the impossible, which here is the prior circularity). Divisions of refutation: Refutation is divided into two types: The first type: (real refutation), which is the rejection of the proof without detailing its two premises. This type is also called (summary refutation), and its target is the prover's proof. This type is divided into two sections: A – the famous refutation. B – the broken refutation. The second type: (semblance refutation), which is the nullification of the claim by testimony of specific corruption, such as contradicting the consensus of scholars, or conflicting with the prover's doctrine, or the like.

Commentary
This passage opens with an example of a shahid (witness) that demonstrates a refutation: the proof of the prover leads to an impossibility, specifically a prior circularity (dawr sabqi). The text then begins a formal classification of naqd (refutation). Refutation is divided into two main types. The first is real refutation (naqd haqiqi), which is a direct rejection of the proof without analyzing its premises individually; it is also called summary refutation (naqd ijmaali). Its target is the prover's proof itself. Real refutation is further subdivided into famous refutation (naqd mashhur) and broken refutation (naqd maksur). The second main type is semblance refutation (naqd shibhi), which attacks the claim rather than the proof, using evidence of specific flaws such as contradiction with scholarly consensus or the prover's own stated position. This classification sets the stage for more detailed explanation.

Real Refutation (Naqd Haqiqi) and Its Subcategories
Defines naqd and divides it into haqiqi and shibhi with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between famous (mashhur) and broken (maksur) refutation, and explain how a shahid (witness) demonstrates a refutation through prior circularity.

Core Idea
Real refutation destroys the whole proof at once—either famously (obvious flaw) or brokenly (specific fatal objection).

Real refutation (naqd haqiqi) is a powerful tool: it rejects the prover's proof without examining its internal premises. This lesson delves into its subcategories—famous and broken—and uses the example of prior circularity (dawr sabqi) as a shahid that proves the proof leads to impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of naqd haqiqi
It is the rejection of the proof as a whole, without detailing its premises. Also called summary refutation (naqd ijmaali).
2
Identify the two subcategories
Al-naqd al-mashhur (famous): the flaw is well-known and immediately recognized. Al-naqd al-maksur (broken): the flaw is specific and breaks the proof in that particular case.
The passage lists them but does not elaborate; key terms to remember.
3
Analyze the shahid example
The passage gives a shahid (witness) of prior circularity: the prover's proof requires the thing to exist before it exists—a self-evident impossibility. This demonstrates that the proof is corrupt.
The shahid is not the refutation itself but the evidence that the proof entails impossibility.
4
Distinguish from naqd shibhi
While naqd haqiqi attacks the proof structurally, naqd shibhi attacks the claim using external contradictions (e.g., consensus). Ensure you can tell them apart.
Practice: given a refutation, decide if it is haqiqi or shibhi.

Famous vs Broken Refutation in Dialectic
Explains real naqd and its two subtypes with conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a famous refutation (naqd mashhur) and a broken refutation (naqd maksur) based on whether the questioner presents the prover's proof completely or with omissions.

Core Idea
A famous refutation preserves the proof; a broken refutation alters it.

In jadal (dialectical debate), the questioner may expose a flaw in the prover's proof either by reproducing it exactly and showing it fails, or by omitting parts of it. The first is called a famous refutation and is always valid if the corruption is shown; the second is called a broken refutation and its validity depends on whether the omitted part is essential. Let's learn how to tell them apart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prover's proof (dall)
Locate the complete argument given by the prover in the debate. It consists of a claim and a reason. In the passage, the prover's proof is the syllogism: 'This shape is a square because it is a surface enclosed by four equal lines...'
2
Check the questioner's presentation
Does the questioner cite the proof word for word (famous) or does he drop some words or phrases (broken)? Famous refutation keeps the entire proof unchanged; broken refutation omits something.
3
Determine the impact of any omission
If the questioner omitted something, ask: would the conclusion still be at risk if the omission were included? If the omission is essential and changes the result, the refutation is invalid; if non-essential, it may be valid.
This step is crucial for broken refutation; for famous refutation, the impact is already shown without omission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح النقض الحقيقي بقسميه المشهور والمكسور: عرفنا أن النقض الحقيقي معناه ردُّ دليل المعلّل بشاهد يثبت فيه السائل صحة النقض، فمَوْرِد النقض في النقض الحقيقي دليلُ المعلّل. وهو قسمان: نقض مشهور، ونقض مكسور. أما النقض المشهور: فهو النقض الذي يورد فيه السائل كلَّ عناصر دليل المعلّل، فلا يترك منها شيئاً، ويورده على هيئته، فلا يغير فيه شيئاً، ولا يحذف منه شيئاً، ويثبت مع ذلك فساده، بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، أو باستلزام الدليل المحال. ونمثل له بما سبق من أمثلة للنقض. وأما النقض المكسور: فهو النقض الذي يورد فيه السائل دليل المعلّل مع حذف بعض العناصر التي اشتمل عليها. فإذا كان ما حذف من الأوصاف يؤثر في تغيير النتيجة، فهو نقض مردود، ولا يجوز ارتكابه في المناظرة، وإذا ارتكبه السائل أجاب عنه المعلّل ببيان ما تركه من الدليل، مع بيان أنه لو لم يتركه لما استقام له توجيه نقضه. وإذا كان ما حذف من الأوصاف لا يؤثر في تغيير النتيجة، فهو نقض مقبول، ويصح معه توجيه النقض. أمثلة للنقض المكسور المردود الذي لا يجوز ارتكابه : أ ـ المعلّل: هذا الشكل مربع، لأنه سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية، وكل سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية فهو مربع. (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدّعاه) السائل : هذا الدليل باطل لأنه يجري على مُدعىً آخر، وهو المستطيل، ومتوازي الأضلاع، والمعين، (فإنه يقال في كل منها: إنه سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط). فحذف السائل من دليل المعلّل كلمة (متساوية)، ولهذه الكلمة مدخل أساسي في صحة دليل المعلّل، وبها يتميز المربع عن الأشكال الهندسية التي أوردها السائل. المعلّل : هذا نقض مكسور تُرِك فيه ما له دخل أساسي في الدليل، وهو كلمة (متساوية)، لذلك فهو نقض مردود. ب ـ المعلّل: محمد بن عبدالله العربي الهاشمي نبي (مدعى)، لأنه ادعى النبوة وأيده الله بالمعجزات، وكل من كان كذلك فهو نبي (دليل أنتج المدعى في نظر المعلّل).
Explanation of real refutation with its two sections, the famous and the broken: We know that real refutation means the rejection of the prover's proof by a witness in which the questioner establishes the validity of the refutation. So the target of refutation in real refutation is the prover's proof. It is two sections: famous refutation and broken refutation. As for the famous refutation: it is the refutation in which the questioner presents all the elements of the prover's proof, leaving nothing out, and presents it in its form, changing nothing, deleting nothing, and still establishes its corruption, either by the signified failing to follow from the proof, or by the proof entailing the impossible. We illustrate it with previous examples of refutation. As for the broken refutation: it is the refutation in which the questioner presents the prover's proof with deletion of some of the elements it contained. If what was deleted from the descriptions affects a change in the result, it is a rejected refutation, and it is not permissible to commit it in debate; if the questioner commits it, the prover responds by pointing out what was left out of the proof, along with explaining that had he not left it out, his refutation would not have held. If what was deleted from the descriptions does not affect a change in the result, it is an accepted refutation, and the direction of refutation is valid with it. Examples of the rejected broken refutation that is not permissible to commit: A – The prover: This shape is a square, because it is a surface enclosed by four equal lines, and every surface enclosed by four equal lines is a square. (A proof that in the view of the prover produced his claim). The questioner: This proof is invalid because it applies to another claim, namely the rectangle, parallelogram, and rhombus (for it is said of each of them that it is a surface enclosed by four lines). So the questioner deleted from the prover's proof the word 'equal', and this word has a fundamental role in the soundness of the prover's proof, and by it the square is distinguished from the geometric shapes the questioner mentioned. The prover: This is a broken refutation in which what has a fundamental role in the proof was left out, namely the word 'equal', therefore it is a rejected refutation. B – The prover: Muhammad bin Abdullah al-Arabi al-Hashimi is a prophet (claim), because he claimed prophethood and Allah supported him with miracles, and everyone who is like that is a prophet (a proof that produced the claim in the view of the prover).
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of real refutation (al-naqd al-haqiqi) and its two subdivisions. The text first reiterates that real refutation involves the questioner presenting a witness (shahid) to show the corruption of the prover's proof. The target is always the proof itself. Then it distinguishes two types: famous refutation (naqd mashhur) and broken refutation (naqd maksur). Famous refutation is when the questioner reproduces the entire proof without any change or omission and still demonstrates its corruption—either because the claimed conclusion does not follow or because the proof leads to an impossibility. The author notes it will illustrate this with previous examples (not shown here). Broken refutation, by contrast, involves the questioner omitting some elements of the proof. The passage then explains the critical condition: if the omitted part affects the outcome, the refutation is invalid and the prover can call it out by showing that the omission undermines the refutation. If the omitted part does not affect the outcome, the refutation is valid. Two examples are given: the first involves a geometric syllogism where the questioner drops the word 'equal' (musawiyya) from the definition of a square, making it applicable to other shapes; the prover correctly rejects this as a broken refutation. The second example is about the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him), but the text cuts off before the questioner's response. The examples demonstrate how a broken refutation fails when it leaves out an essential component of the proof.

Key Takeaway
Real refutation is divided into famous (complete presentation) and broken (with omission); only broken refutation requires checking whether the omitted part is essential.

Evaluating Broken Refutation: Essential vs Non-Essential Omission
Explains real naqd and its two subtypes with conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a broken refutation is valid or invalid by assessing if the omitted part of the prover's proof changes the conclusion.

Core Idea
A broken refutation stands if the omitted description is incidental; it falls if the omitted description is essential.

The passage gives a clear example: a prover defines a square by four equal lines; the questioner drops 'equal' and claims the proof applies to quadrilaterals. The prover rejects this because 'equal' is essential. Let's master this criterion so you can identify valid broken refutations in Islamic debates.

Guided Path
1
Extract the full proof as given by the prover
List all the premises and terms the prover uses. In the square example: 'surface enclosed by four lines' and 'equal' are both present.
2
Identify what the questioner omitted
Compare the questioner's version to the original. The questioner left out the word 'equal'.
3
Assess the effect of the omission
Would the prover's proof still hold for the intended subject without the omitted part? In the square example, without 'equal', the definition applies to any quadrilateral, so the proof no longer distinguishes a square. Therefore the omission is essential and the refutation is invalid.
If the omission does not affect the validity of the proof (e.g., omitting a redundant adjective), the refutation may be valid.

The Broken Refutation: Definition and Types
Author defines broken refutation and its two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a broken refutation as accepted or rejected by examining whether the deleted element affects the conclusion.

Core Idea
A broken refutation is only flawed if the deleted attribute is essential to the argument.

In dialectical debates, the broken refutation (al-naqd al-maksur) occurs when the questioner presents the proponent's argument while omitting some elements. Whether this omission invalidates the refutation depends on the importance of the deleted element. This lesson introduces the definition and its two types: rejected (mardud) and accepted (maqbul).

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of broken refutation
Reread the first two lines of the source. What does the author say the questioner does? The questioner presents the proponent's argument but deletes some of its elements.
2
Identify the two conditions for acceptance vs rejection
The author gives two cases: if the deleted attribute affects the conclusion, the refutation is rejected; if it does not affect the conclusion, it is accepted. Write down the exact wording.
3
Contrast the two outcomes
For the rejected type, the proponent must respond by pointing out the omission; for the accepted type, the refutation stands. Why does this distinction matter in a debate?
4
Test with a simple scenario
Imagine a proponent argues: 'This animal is a cat because it has four legs and a tail.' The questioner deletes 'and a tail'. Would the refutation be accepted or rejected? Justify based on the passage's criterion.

As for the broken refutation: it is the refutation in which the questioner presents the proponent's argument with deletion of some of the elements it contained.
If what was deleted from the attributes affects the change of the conclusion, then it is a rejected refutation, and it is not permissible to commit it in the disputation, and if the questioner commits it, the proponent responds by stating what was left out of the argument, along with stating that had he not left it out, his refutation would not have been sound.
And if what was deleted from the attributes does not affect the change of the conclusion, then it is an accepted refutation, and the refutation is valid with it.
Examples of the rejected broken refutation which is not permissible to commit:
A – The proponent: This shape is a square, because it is a surface surrounded by four equal lines, and every surface surrounded by four equal lines is a square.
(The argument produced the claim in the proponent's view)
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim, namely the rectangle, the parallelogram, and the rhombus (for it is said of each of them: it is a surface surrounded by four lines).
So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument the word (equal), and this word has a fundamental role in the validity of the proponent's argument, and by it the square is distinguished from the geometric shapes that the questioner mentioned.
The proponent: This is a broken refutation in which what has a fundamental role in the argument was omitted, namely the word (equal), therefore it is a rejected refutation.
B – The proponent: Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Arabi al-Hashimi is a prophet (claim), because he claimed prophethood and God supported him with miracles, and everyone who is like that is a prophet (the argument produced the claim in the proponent's view).
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim who is not a prophet, such as Musaylima the liar, for he claimed prophethood. (So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument 'and God supported him with miracles', and this condition has a fundamental role in the validity of the proponent's argument, and by it the prophet is distinguished from the false claimant to prophethood.)
The proponent: This is a rejected broken refutation, because what has a fundamental role in the argument was omitted, namely the condition (and God supported him with miracles).
Examples of the accepted broken refutation:
A – The proponent: This universe is eternal (claim), because it is an effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him in its existence, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him in its existence is eternal.
(The argument produced the claim in the proponent's view)
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim, namely the daily events, for it is said of them: they are an effect of the Eternal, while they are temporally originated by necessity.
(So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument the phrase 'and dependent on Him in its existence', but this phrase adds nothing to the first part of the argument, which is 'because it is an effect of the Eternal', so deleting it or mentioning it does not affect the conclusion.) So it is a broken refutation, but it is accepted.
B – The proponent: This one is afraid (claim), because he has a yellow face, and sits on his bed, and in his hand is a pen.
And everyone who is like that is afraid.
(The argument produced the claim in the proponent's view).
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim, namely the patient whose face is yellow from illness, for it is also said of him: yellow face.
(So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument the phrase 'and sits on his bed and in his hand'
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the 'broken refutation' (al-naqd al-maksur) in dialectical disputation. The author begins by stating that the broken refutation is one in which the questioner (al-sa'il) presents the proponent's (al-mu'allil) evidence while intentionally deleting some of its elements. Two types are then distinguished based on the effect of the deletion. If the deleted attribute(s) would change the conclusion, the refutation is rejected (mardud) and impermissible, and if the questioner commits it, the proponent must point out what was omitted and explain that without it the refutation is unsound. If the deletion does not affect the conclusion, the refutation is accepted (maqbul) and valid.

Two examples illustrate the rejected type. In the first, the proponent argues that a shape is a square because it is a surface bounded by four equal lines. The questioner deletes the word 'equal', claiming the argument would also apply to rectangles, parallelograms, and rhombi. The proponent rejects this because 'equal' is essential to the definition of a square. In the second example, the proponent argues that Muhammad (peace be upon him) is a prophet because he claimed prophethood and was supported by miracles. The questioner deletes the miracle condition, citing Musaylima the liar who also claimed prophethood. The proponent again rejects the refutation because the miracles are the differentiating factor.

For the accepted type, the first example involves a proponent claiming the universe is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him for existence. The questioner deletes the dependency phrase but retains 'effect of the Eternal'. Since 'effect of the Eternal' already entails dependency, the deletion does not change the conclusion, so the refutation is accepted. The second example is cut off in the text but follows the same pattern: the proponent argues someone is afraid based on yellow face, sitting on a bed, and holding a pen; the questioner deletes the last two attributes but the conclusion's correctness or fallacy likely hinges on whether those attributes matter. The incomplete passage suggests the author was providing a parallel illustration.

Key Terms
النقض المكسور (al-naqd al-maksur): A refutation in which the questioner presents the proponent's argument but deletes some of its elements; it may be accepted or rejected depending on whether the deletion affects the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift — this term is stable in the jadal literature.]
النقض (al-naqd): An objection or refutation in debate; here specifically the act of the questioner showing that the proponent's argument would apply to an unintended case.
المعلّل (al-mu'allil): The proponent who provides an argument (dahl) to support a claim; the one being challenged.
السائل (al-sa'il): The questioner who raises objections or refutations against the proponent's argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a debate, the proponent argues: 'Zaid is a prophet because he claimed prophethood and performed miracles.' The questioner objects: 'This argument is false because it also applies to Musaylima, who claimed prophethood.' What type of broken refutation is this, and why?
A) Accepted, because the objection correctly identifies a common attribute.
B) Rejected, because the questioner deleted the condition of miracles, which is essential.
C) Accepted, because Musaylima indeed claimed prophethood and the argument applies to him.
D) Rejected, because the questioner did not delete any element from the original argument.

Key Takeaway
The broken refutation is accepted or rejected based solely on whether the deleted element is essential to the argument's conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following exchange: Proponent: 'This liquid is intoxicating because it is made from grapes and ferments. Anyone who is like that is intoxicating.' Questioner: 'Then wine is not intoxicating because the argument also applies to grape juice that ferments only briefly.' Identify the deletion and decide if the refutation is accepted or rejected.
?
In a debate on whether Zakat is obligatory on camels, the proponent cites a hadith with a condition. The questioner omits that condition. Classify the broken refutation and explain your reasoning.
