Examples of Broken Refutations
Author provides examples of rejected and accepted broken refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify essential and non-essential elements in an argument and apply the criteria to evaluate broken refutations in given examples.

Core Idea
The square example and the prophet example illustrate rejected refutations; the universe example shows an accepted one.

In this lesson, we examine concrete examples from the text to see how the rule of broken refutation applies. By working through these examples, you will practice distinguishing essential from non-essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Restate the proponent's full argument for each example
For the prophet example, write down all attributes: 'claimed prophethood and was supported by miracles'. For the universe example: 'effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him in existence'.
2
Identify the questioner's deletion
What did the questioner omit? In the prophet example, he deleted 'and was supported by miracles'. In the universe example, he deleted 'and dependent on Him in existence'.
3
Check if the deletion is essential
Ask: if the deleted attribute were kept, would the objection still apply? For the prophet example, without miracles the argument applies to false prophets; with it, it does not. Hence essential. For the universe example, 'effect of the Eternal' already implies dependence, so the deletion doesn't change the objection.
4
Classify the refutation
Based on step 3, classify each as rejected (essential deletion) or accepted (non-essential deletion).

ب ـ المعلّل: هذا خائف (مدعى)، لأنه أصفر الوجه، ويجلس على سريره، وبيده قلم.

And we illustrate it with the previous examples of refutation.
As for the broken refutation: it is the refutation in which the questioner presents the proponent's argument with deletion of some of the elements it contained.
If what was deleted from the attributes affects the change of the conclusion, then it is a rejected refutation, and it is not permissible to commit it in the disputation, and if the questioner commits it, the proponent responds by stating what was left out of the argument, along with stating that had he not left it out, his refutation would not have been sound.
And if what was deleted from the attributes does not affect the change of the conclusion, then it is an accepted refutation, and the refutation is valid with it.
Examples of the rejected broken refutation which is not permissible to commit:
A – The proponent: This shape is a square, because it is a surface surrounded by four equal lines, and every surface surrounded by four equal lines is a square.
(The argument produced the claim in the proponent's view)
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim, namely the rectangle, the parallelogram, and the rhombus (for it is said of each of them: it is a surface surrounded by four lines).
So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument the word (equal), and this word has a fundamental role in the validity of the proponent's argument, and by it the square is distinguished from the geometric shapes that the questioner mentioned.
The proponent: This is a broken refutation in which what has a fundamental role in the argument was omitted, namely the word (equal), therefore it is a rejected refutation.
B – The proponent: Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Arabi al-Hashimi is a prophet (claim), because he claimed prophethood and God supported him with miracles, and everyone who is like that is a prophet (the argument produced the claim in the proponent's view).
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim who is not a prophet, such as Musaylima the liar, for he claimed prophethood. (So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument 'and God supported him with miracles', and this condition has a fundamental role in the validity of the proponent's argument, and by it the prophet is distinguished from the false claimant to prophethood.)
The proponent: This is a rejected broken refutation, because what has a fundamental role in the argument was omitted, namely the condition (and God supported him with miracles).
Examples of the accepted broken refutation:
A – The proponent: This universe is eternal (claim), because it is an effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him in its existence, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him in its existence is eternal.
(The argument produced the claim in the proponent's view)
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim, namely the daily events, for it is said of them: they are an effect of the Eternal, while they are temporally originated by necessity.
(So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument the phrase 'and dependent on Him in its existence', but this phrase adds nothing to the first part of the argument, which is 'because it is an effect of the Eternal', so deleting it or mentioning it does not affect the conclusion.) So it is a broken refutation, but it is accepted.
B – The proponent: This one is afraid (claim), because he has a yellow face, and sits on his bed, and in his hand is a pen.
And everyone who is like that is afraid.
(The argument produced the claim in the proponent's view).
The questioner: This argument is false because it applies to another claim, namely the patient whose face is yellow from illness, for it is also said of him: yellow face.
(So the questioner deleted from the proponent's argument the phrase 'and sits on his bed and in his hand'
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the 'broken refutation' (al-naqd al-maksur) in dialectical disputation. The author begins by stating that the broken refutation is one in which the questioner (al-sa'il) presents the proponent's (al-mu'allil) evidence while intentionally deleting some of its elements. Two types are then distinguished based on the effect of the deletion. If the deleted attribute(s) would change the conclusion, the refutation is rejected (mardud) and impermissible, and if the questioner commits it, the proponent must point out what was omitted and explain that without it the refutation is unsound. If the deletion does not affect the conclusion, the refutation is accepted (maqbul) and valid.

Two examples illustrate the rejected type. In the first, the proponent argues that a shape is a square because it is a surface bounded by four equal lines. The questioner deletes the word 'equal', claiming the argument would also apply to rectangles, parallelograms, and rhombi. The proponent rejects this because 'equal' is essential to the definition of a square. In the second example, the proponent argues that Muhammad (peace be upon him) is a prophet because he claimed prophethood and was supported by miracles. The questioner deletes the miracle condition, citing Musaylima the liar who also claimed prophethood. The proponent again rejects the refutation because the miracles are the differentiating factor.

For the accepted type, the first example involves a proponent claiming the universe is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal and dependent on Him for existence. The questioner deletes the dependency phrase but retains 'effect of the Eternal'. Since 'effect of the Eternal' already entails dependency, the deletion does not change the conclusion, so the refutation is accepted. The second example is cut off in the text but follows the same pattern: the proponent argues someone is afraid based on yellow face, sitting on a bed, and holding a pen; the questioner deletes the last two attributes but the conclusion's correctness or fallacy likely hinges on whether those attributes matter. The incomplete passage suggests the author was providing a parallel illustration.

Key Terms
النقض المكسور (al-naqd al-maksur): A refutation in which the questioner presents the proponent's argument but deletes some of its elements; it may be accepted or rejected depending on whether the deletion affects the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift — this term is stable in the jadal literature.]
النقض (al-naqd): An objection or refutation in debate; here specifically the act of the questioner showing that the proponent's argument would apply to an unintended case.
المعلّل (al-mu'allil): The proponent who provides an argument (dahl) to support a claim; the one being challenged.
السائل (al-sa'il): The questioner who raises objections or refutations against the proponent's argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a debate, the proponent argues: 'Zaid is a prophet because he claimed prophethood and performed miracles.' The questioner objects: 'This argument is false because it also applies to Musaylima, who claimed prophethood.' What type of broken refutation is this, and why?
A) Accepted, because the objection correctly identifies a common attribute.
B) Rejected, because the questioner deleted the condition of miracles, which is essential.
C) Accepted, because Musaylima indeed claimed prophethood and the argument applies to him.
D) Rejected, because the questioner did not delete any element from the original argument.

Key Takeaway
A broken refutation is accepted only when the deleted element is redundant; essential deletions are always rejected.

?

?

The Broken Refutation: When Omitting a Premise Succeeds or Fails
Author defines broken refutation and gives two accepted examples with analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a rejected and an accepted broken refutation (naqd maksur) by identifying whether the omitted part of the argument was essential or non-essential.

Core Idea
A refutation that omits a part of the opponent's argument is broken, but viable if the omission is irrelevant.

In dialectical debate, a common tactic is to refute an argument by showing it applies to another case. But if you omit a premise from the original argument, the refutation is 'broken'. The key question is: Was the omitted premise essential to the original proof? This lesson examines two examples from the text to see when a broken refutation is rejected and when it is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument (mu'allil's proof)
Find the full proof presented by the defendant. Note all premises and conditions explicitly stated.
In the first example, the proof includes the condition 'supported by miracles'.
2
Identify the questioner's objection and what was omitted
The questioner (sa'il) attempts to refute by applying the same reasoning to a different case. Determine which part of the original proof they left out.
In the first example, the questioner omitted 'supported by miracles'.
3
Determine whether the omitted part was essential
Assess if the omitted premise is necessary for the original conclusion. If the argument collapses without it, it is essential. If the conclusion still holds without it, it is non-essential.
In the first example, the condition 'supported by miracles' is essential because it distinguishes a true prophet from a false claimant.
4
Classify the refutation as rejected or accepted
If the omitted part was essential, the broken refutation is rejected (mardud). If non-essential, it is accepted (maqbul).
The first example is rejected; the second and third examples are accepted.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فحذف السائل من دليل المعلّل عبارة «ويجلس على سريره وبيده قلم»، ولكن هذه العبارة لا مدخل لها في الدليل لدى التأمل، إذ لا يدل الجلوس على السرير والإمساك بالقلم على الخوف، فحذفها أو ذكرها لا يؤثر في النتيجة). إذن فهو نقض مكسور ولكنه مقبول.
The questioner: This proof is false, because it applies to another claimant who is not a prophet, like Musaylima the liar, for he claimed prophethood. (So the questioner omitted from the proof of the defendant 'And God supported him with miracles', and this condition has an essential role in the validity of the defendant's proof, and by it the prophet is distinguished from the false prophet.) (Proof that produced, in the defendant's view, his claim). (So the questioner omitted from the defendant's proof the phrase 'and sits on his bed and holds a pen', but this phrase has no role in the proof upon reflection, because sitting on the bed and holding a pen do not indicate fear, so omitting it or mentioning it does not affect the result). Thus it is a broken refutation but it is accepted.

Commentary
This passage explains a type of dialectical refutation called al-naqd al-maksur (broken refutation). The author begins with a hypothetical exchange: The questioner attacks the defendant's proof that a claimant to prophethood is a true prophet, by omitting the crucial condition 'supported by miracles'. The defendant points out that this omission renders the refutation invalid because it removes an essential element. The author then gives two detailed examples of accepted broken refutations. In the first example, the defendant argues for the eternity of the universe using a syllogism. The questioner tries to refute it by applying the same argument to temporal events, but he drops the phrase 'dependent in its existence on Him'. However, this phrase is not essential to the argument; the core is 'effect of the Eternal'. So the refutation is technically broken (since it omitted something) but it is accepted because the omitted part was irrelevant. The second example involves a claim that someone is afraid based on facial yellowness, sitting, and holding a pen. The questioner applies the same reasoning to a sick person, but omits the sitting and pen-holding. Since those attributes are not actually indicative of fear, the refutation is broken but acceptable. The key insight is that a refutation is 'broken' when it omits a premise or condition from the original argument, but it may still be valid if that omitted element was unnecessary. This lesson teaches the subtle distinction between essential and non-essential parts of an argument in dialectical practice.

Key Takeaway
A broken refutation is accepted only if the omitted part of the proof is non-essential; otherwise it is rejected.

Understanding Analogical Refutation: Definition and Key Components
Defines analogical refutation and lists its possible corruption types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define analogical refutation and identify its essential components (specific corruption, witness) in a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Analogical refutation destroys a claim by exposing a specific corruption in its proof.

In dialectical debate, a simple denial is not enough; you need a sharp tool. Analogical refutation (al-naqd al-shabihi) is that tool. It invalidates the opponent's claim by showing a specific corruption (fasad makhsus) in their proof, such as contradiction with scholarly consensus (ijma') or the opponent's own school. This passage defines it and sets the stage for its application. Let's break down its structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Look for the mudda'i's assertion. In the example, it's 'Hiring a worker for an uncertain fee is invalid.'
2
Find the proof (dalil) provided
What reasoning supports the claim? The proof: because it is a transaction containing gharar, and every such transaction is invalid.
3
Locate the specific corruption (fasad makhsus)
The questioner points to a specific flaw: the consensus of scholars (ijma') on the permissibility of mudaraba, which has similar uncertainty. This shows the proof's generalization is false.
4
Understand the 'witness' (shahid)
The witness is what the questioner brings to demonstrate the corruption. Here, it's the ijma'.
5
Recognize the effect
The refutation invalidates the claim because the proof fails to hold universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح النقض الشبيهي: عرفنا أن النقض الشبيهي هو إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد مخصوص، كالمخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو المنافاة لمذهب المعلّل، ونحو ذلك. والشاهد هنا هو ما يبينه السائل من الفساد المخصوص، كإجماع العلماء على تخلف المدلول عن الدليل في صورة النقض، وككون ما استدل به المعلّل لا يصح دليلاً في مذهبه، وإليك الأمثلة الموضحة: أمثلة للنقض الشبيهي: أ ـ المعلّل الفقيه: لا يجوز استئجار العامل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق مجهولة المقدار (المدّعى)، لأنها معاوضة بما فيه غرر، وكل معاوضة من هذا القبيل فاسدة. (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدعاه) السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه في شركة (القراض)(١) فهي من قبيل المعاوضة على العمل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق، لاحتمال الربح والخسارة، ومجهولة المقدار المعين، لأن الثلث أو النصف لا يعرف كم يكون إلا بعد حصوله، فتحقق الربح مجهول، ومقدار الربح مجهول. (نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل بسبب الفساد المخصوص، الذي هو هنا إجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه، في صورة النقض التي (١) هي الشركة التي يكون المال فيها من أحد الشريكين، والعمل من الشريك الآخر، على أن يكون الربح بينهما؛ لصاحب المال حصة، ولصاحب العمل حصة، بحسب اتفاقهما. وأما الخسارة: فصاحب المال يخسر من ماله، وصاحب العمل يخسر ما بذل من جهد.
Explanation of the analogical refutation: We have known that the analogical refutation is invalidating the claim by witness of a specific corruption, like contradiction of the consensus of scholars, or opposition to the defendant's school, and the like. And the witness here is what the questioner shows of the specific corruption, like the consensus of scholars on the non-fulfillment of the indicated (conclusion) from the proof in the form of the refutation, and like that what the defendant used as proof is not valid as a proof in his own school. And here are the clarifying examples: Examples of the analogical refutation: A — The jurist defendant: It is not permissible to hire a worker for a fee that is uncertain in realization and uncertain in amount (the claim), because it is a transaction containing gharar (uncertainty), and every transaction of this sort is invalid. (Proof that produced, in the defendant's view, his claim). The questioner: This proof is refuted by the consensus of scholars on the non-fulfillment of its requirement in the mudaraba partnership, for it is a type of transaction for work with a fee uncertain in realization, due to the possibility of profit and loss, and uncertain in definite amount, because the third or half is not known except after its occurrence. So the realization of profit is unknown, and the amount of profit is unknown. (Analogical refutation by which the questioner invalidated the defendant's proof because of the specific corruption, which here is the consensus of scholars on the non-fulfillment of its requirement, in the form of the refutation which (1) ...). Footnote: (1) It is the partnership in which the capital comes from one partner, and the work from the other partner, such that the profit is between them; the owner of capital has a share, and the owner of work has a share, according to their agreement. As for loss: the owner of capital loses from his money, and the owner of work loses what he exerted of effort.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage introduces the analogical refutation (al-naqd al-shabihi), which is a method of invalidating a claim by demonstrating a specific corruption (fasad makhsus) in the argument. The author explains that this corruption can be, for example, a contradiction with scholarly consensus (ijma') or with the defendant's own doctrinal position. The key is that the questioner points to a particular instance where the defendant's proof fails according to an accepted standard. The author illustrates this with a fiqh example: a jurist argues that hiring a worker with an uncertain wage is invalid because it involves gharar (uncertainty). The questioner responds by citing the consensus of scholars that the mudaraba (profit-sharing partnership) is permissible, even though it involves similar uncertainty — the worker's share of profit is unknown in amount and realization. This demonstrates that the defendant's proof does not hold universally, thus it is refuted. The passage shows how analogical refutation operates by using a parallel case that undermines the generality of the proof. This is a standard dialectical technique in Islamic legal reasoning.

Key Terms
نقض شبيهي
فساد مخصوص
إجماع
معاوضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'specific corruption' that the questioner used to refute the defendant's argument?
A) The defendant's argument contradicts the principle of gharar.
B) The defendant's argument contradicts the consensus (ijma') on the permissibility of mudaraba.
C) The defendant's school does not accept the proof.
D) The fee in the hiring contract was actually certain.

Key Takeaway
Analogical refutation uses a specific corruption like ijma' to show that the opponent's proof fails universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the claim, proof, and specific corruption in the following dialectical scenario: A jurist claims that selling dates before they ripen is invalid because it involves uncertainty (gharar). The opponent refutes by citing the consensus on the permissibility of selling fruit before it ripens under certain conditions.
?
Construct your own analogical refutation against the claim: 'All forms of speculation in business are prohibited because they involve uncertainty.' Use an Islamic finance example like murabaha.

Analyzing the Mudaraba Example: How Analogical Refutation Works in Practice
Illustrates analogical refutation through a mudaraba example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the steps of an analogical refutation in a fiqh example and identify how consensus serves as a specific corruption.

Core Idea
A single counterexample grounded in consensus can collapse a universal proof.

The passage provides a concrete example of analogical refutation from fiqh. A jurist argues that hiring with an uncertain fee is invalid because it involves gharar. The questioner refutes by pointing to mudaraba, which is permitted by consensus despite similar uncertainty. Let's dissect this example to see the mechanism.

Guided Path
1
State the defendant's claim and proof
Claim: Hiring with an uncertain fee is invalid. Proof: It's a transaction with gharar, and all such transactions are invalid.
2
Identify the parallel case (naqd)
The questioner brings up mudaraba (profit-sharing partnership), which is a transaction with uncertain fee for the worker.
3
Verify the consensus (ijma')
The questioner asserts that scholars agree on the permissibility of mudaraba.
4
Apply the refutation
Since mudaraba is permitted despite having the same feature (uncertainty), the defendant's generalization is false. The proof is 'broken' by this specific corruption.
5
Conclude the dialectical outcome
The defendant's argument is invalidated; the claim does not follow from the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح النقض الشبيهي: عرفنا أن النقض الشبيهي هو إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد مخصوص، كالمخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو المنافاة لمذهب المعلّل، ونحو ذلك. والشاهد هنا هو ما يبينه السائل من الفساد المخصوص، كإجماع العلماء على تخلف المدلول عن الدليل في صورة النقض، وككون ما استدل به المعلّل لا يصح دليلاً في مذهبه، وإليك الأمثلة الموضحة: أمثلة للنقض الشبيهي: أ ـ المعلّل الفقيه: لا يجوز استئجار العامل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق مجهولة المقدار (المدّعى)، لأنها معاوضة بما فيه غرر، وكل معاوضة من هذا القبيل فاسدة. (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدعاه) السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه في شركة (القراض)(١) فهي من قبيل المعاوضة على العمل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق، لاحتمال الربح والخسارة، ومجهولة المقدار المعين، لأن الثلث أو النصف لا يعرف كم يكون إلا بعد حصوله، فتحقق الربح مجهول، ومقدار الربح مجهول. Defining the Resemblance Refutation
Definition of naqd shabihi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a naqd shabihi: claim, evidence, specific corruption, and witness.

Core Idea
Naqd shabihi targets a precise flaw in the evidence, not the entire argument.

In dialectical debate, not all refutations are created equal. The naqd shabihi targets a specific corruption in the arguer's evidence, such as inconsistency with consensus or the arguer's own school. This lesson unpacks the definition and structure of this powerful refutation tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and evidence
Locate the arguer's claim (da'wa) and the evidence (dalil) they present. The refutation will attack the evidence, not the claim directly.
In the examples, the claim is a ruling and the evidence is a general principle.
2
Find the specific corruption (fasad makhsus)
The questioner points out a precise flaw: either the evidence contradicts consensus (ijma') or contradicts the arguer's own doctrine (madhhab).
This corruption must be specific to the case at hand.
3
Understand the role of the witness (shahid)
The witness is the evidence the questioner brings to demonstrate the corruption. It shows that the arguer's evidence fails in a parallel case.
In the first example, the qirad company serves as the witness.
4
Recognize the two types of corruption
The source mentions two: contravening consensus and contradicting the arguer's own madhhab. These are the most common in jadal.
Other types may exist, but these are foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح النقض الشبيهي:
عرفنا أن النقض الشبيهي هو إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد مخصوص، كالمخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو المنافاة لمذهب المعلّل، ونحو ذلك.
والشاهد هنا هو ما يبينه السائل من الفساد المخصوص، كإجماع العلماء على تخلف المدلول عن الدليل في صورة النقض، وككون ما استدل به المعلّل لا يصح دليلاً في مذهبه، وإليك الأمثلة الموضحة:
أمثلة للنقض الشبيهي:
أ ـ المعلّل الفقيه: لا يجوز استئجار العامل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق مجهولة المقدار (المدّعى)، لأنها معاوضة بما فيه غرر، وكل معاوضة من هذا القبيل فاسدة.
(دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدعاه)
السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه في شركة (القراض)(١) فهي من قبيل المعاوضة على العمل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق، لاحتمال الربح والخسارة، ومجهولة المقدار المعين، لأن الثلث أو النصف لا يعرف كم يكون إلا بعد حصوله، فتحقق الربح مجهول، ومقدار الربح مجهول.
(نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل بسبب الفساد المخصوص، الذي هو هنا إجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه، في صورة النقض التي أوردها وهي شركة القراض. إذن فالدليل مجمع على خلافه في صورة النقض).
ب ـ المعلّل الفقيه الشافعي والمالكي: لا يحرم نكاح الزانية (المدعى).
لأن قول الله تعالى: ﴿الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زانٍ أو مشرك وحُرِّم ذلك على المؤمنين﴾ منسوخ بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وأنكحوا الأيامى منكم ﴾.
(دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدّعاه)
السائل: هذا دليل منقوض، لأن الناسخ فيه أعم من المنسوخ، إذ لفظ (الأيامى) يشملُ العفائف والزواني والذكور والإناث، ولا يجوز في مذهب المعلل نسخ الخاص بالعام، فهو دليل باطل بمقتضى مذهب المعلّل.
(نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل، بسبب فساد مخصوص، هو هنا اعتماد المعلّل على ما يخالف مذهبه).
(١) هي الشركة التي يكون المال فيها من أحد الشريكين، والعمل من الشريك الآخر، على أن يكون الربح بينهما؛ لصاحب المال حصة، ولصاحب العمل حصة، بحسب اتفاقهما. وأما الخسارة: فصاحب المال يخسر من ماله، وصاحب العمل يخسر ما بذل من جهد.
Explanation of the resemblance refutation: We have known that the resemblance refutation is the invalidation of the claim by testimony of a specific corruption, like contravening the consensus of scholars, or contradicting the doctrine of the arguer, and the like. The evidence here is what the questioner shows of the specific corruption, such as the consensus of scholars on the nonoccurrence of the implication from the evidence in the case of the refutation, and such as the arguer's evidence not being valid as a proof in his own doctrine. Here are clarifying examples: Examples of the resemblance refutation:  A) The arguer jurist: It is not permissible to hire a worker for wages whose realization and amount are unknown (the claim), because it is a transaction involving gharar, and every such transaction is invalid. (An evidence that, in the arguer's view, produces his claim). The questioner: This evidence is refuted by the consensus of scholars on the non-occurrence of its implication in the company of qirad, for it is a type of transaction for work with wages whose realization is unknown, due to the possibility of profit and loss, and of unknown specific amount, because one-third or one-half is not known how much it will be except after it occurs. Thus the realization of profit is unknown, and the amount of profit is unknown. (A resemblance refutation by which the questioner nullified the arguer's evidence due to the specific corruption, which here is the consensus of scholars on the non-occurrence of its implication in the case of the refutation that he presented, i.e., the qirad company. Thus the evidence is disagreed with by consensus in the case of the refutation). B) The arguer jurist (Shafi'i and Maliki): The marriage of a fornicatress is not prohibited (the claim). Because the saying of Allah Most High: "The fornicatress shall not be married except to a fornicator or idolater, and that is forbidden to the believers" is abrogated by His saying: "And marry the unmarried among you." (An evidence that, in the arguer's view, produces his claim). The questioner: This evidence is refuted, because the abrogator is more general than the abrogated; indeed the term 'al-ayama' includes chaste women, fornicatresses, males and females, and it is not permissible in the arguer's doctrine to abrogate the specific with the general. So it is an invalid evidence according to the arguer's doctrine. (A resemblance refutation by which the questioner nullified the arguer's evidence, due to a specific corruption, which here is the arguer's reliance on what contradicts his own doctrine). (1) It is the company in which the capital is from one of the two partners, and the work is from the other partner, on the condition that the profit is between them; the owner of the capital has a share, and the worker has a share, according to their agreement. As for the loss: the owner of the capital loses from his capital, and the worker loses what he expended of effort.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the 'resemblance refutation' (al-naqd al-shabihi). The author begins by stating that it is the invalidation of a claim by pointing out a specific corruption (fasad makhsus) in the argument, such as contradicting scholarly consensus (ijma') or contradicting the arguer's own doctrinal school (madhhab). The 'witness' (shahid) is the evidence the questioner brings to show that corruption. Two examples are given. In the first, a jurist argues that hiring a worker with unknown wages is invalid due to gharar. The questioner refutes by pointing to the qirad company (mudaraba), where profit/ loss is unknown yet it is valid by consensus. Thus the arguer's evidence fails in that case. In the second, a Shafi'i/Maliki jurist claims that marrying a fornicatress is not forbidden, citing an abrogated verse. The questioner notes that the abrogator (general) is more general than the abrogated (specific), which violates the arguer's own principle that a general cannot abrogate a specific. Both examples show how the refutation highlights a specific flaw in the arguer's evidence or its consistency with his own doctrine.

Key Terms
نقض شبيهي (naqd shabihi): A refutation that invalidates a claim by demonstrating a specific corruption in the evidence, such as contradicting consensus or the arguer's own doctrine. [Note: Stable term in jadal literature; no significant shift.]
فساد مخصوص (fasad makhsus): A specific flaw or corruption in the argument, such as inconsistency with consensus or the arguer's own school.
إجماع (ijma'): Scholarly consensus, used here as a criterion for validity or invalidity of an argument in dialectical refutation. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ijma' is a source of law; here it serves as a standard for argument soundness.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the differentia in the definition of naqd shabihi?
A) Invalidation of the claim
B) Testimony of a specific corruption
C) Contravening scholarly consensus
D) Contradicting the arguer's doctrine

Key Takeaway
Naqd shabihi is a targeted refutation that exposes a specific corruption in the arguer's evidence, making it a precise dialectical tool.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a definition of naqd shabihi in your own words, including the two types of fasad makhsus mentioned in the passage.
?
Identify a potential naqd shabihi in a hypothetical debate: a Hanafi jurist claims that selling debt for debt is invalid because it involves gharar. What specific corruption could a questioner use? (Hint: Consider the practice of salam or istisna'a).

Applying Naqd Shabihi: Two Fiqh Examples
Two examples of naqd shabihi in fiqh debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange and determine whether a naqd shabihi has been successfully applied.

Core Idea
A valid naqd shabihi requires that the specific corruption directly contradicts the arguer's own standards.

To master the naqd shabihi, one must see it in action. The two fiqh examples provided in the source illustrate how a questioner can invalidate a jurist's claim by pointing out a specific contradiction. In this lesson, we will walk through each example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first example: hiring with unknown wages
The arguer claims it is invalid due to gharar. The questioner refutes by pointing to the qirad (mudaraba) contract, which also involves unknown wages yet is valid by consensus.
The specific corruption is that consensus contradicts the arguer's evidence.
2
Identify the specific corruption in the first example
The questioner uses the consensus of scholars on the validity of qirad to show that the arguer's evidence (that gharar invalidates) does not apply universally.
The witness (shahid) is the qirad company.
3
Analyze the second example: marrying a fornicatress
The arguer (Shafi'i/Maliki) claims it is not prohibited, using an abrogation argument. The questioner notes that the abrogator is general, which conflicts with the arguer's own principle that a general cannot abrogate a specific.
The corruption is the inconsistency with the arguer's own madhhab.
4
Compare the two types of corruption
The first uses consensus, the second uses the arguer's own doctrine. Both are specific corruptions that invalidate the evidence.
This shows the two main categories of fasad makhsus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عرفنا أن النقض الشبيهي هو إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد مخصوص، كالمخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو المنافاة لمذهب المعلّل، ونحو ذلك.
والشاهد هنا هو ما يبينه السائل من الفساد المخصوص، كإجماع العلماء على تخلف المدلول عن الدليل في صورة النقض، وككون ما استدل به المعلّل لا يصح دليلاً في مذهبه، وإليك الأمثلة الموضحة:

أ ـ المعلّل الفقيه: لا يجوز استئجار العامل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق مجهولة المقدار (المدّعى)، لأنها معاوضة بما فيه غرر، وكل معاوضة من هذا القبيل فاسدة.
(دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدعاه)
السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه في شركة (القراض)(١) فهي من قبيل المعاوضة على العمل بأجرة مجهولة التحقق، لاحتمال الربح والخسارة، ومجهولة المقدار المعين، لأن الثلث أو النصف لا يعرف كم يكون إلا بعد حصوله، فتحقق الربح مجهول، ومقدار الربح مجهول.
(نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل بسبب الفساد المخصوص، الذي هو هنا إجماع العلماء على تخلف مقتضاه، في صورة النقض التي أوردها وهي شركة القراض. إذن فالدليل مجمع على خلافه في صورة النقض).
ب ـ المعلّل الفقيه الشافعي والمالكي: لا يحرم نكاح الزانية (المدعى).
لأن قول الله تعالى: ﴿الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زانٍ أو مشرك وحُرِّم ذلك على المؤمنين﴾ منسوخ بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وأنكحوا الأيامى منكم ﴾.
(دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدّعاه)
السائل: هذا دليل منقوض، لأن الناسخ فيه أعم من المنسوخ، إذ لفظ (الأيامى) يشملُ العفائف والزواني والذكور والإناث، ولا يجوز في مذهب المعلل نسخ الخاص بالعام، فهو دليل باطل بمقتضى مذهب المعلّل.
(نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل، بسبب فساد مخصوص، هو هنا اعتماد المعلّل على ما يخالف مذهبه).
(١) هي الشركة التي يكون المال فيها من أحد الشريكين، والعمل من الشريك الآخر، على أن يكون الربح بينهما؛ لصاحب المال حصة، ولصاحب العمل حصة، بحسب اتفاقهما. وأما الخسارة: فصاحب المال يخسر من ماله، وصاحب العمل يخسر ما بذل من جهد.
Explanation of the resemblance refutation: We have known that the resemblance refutation is the invalidation of the claim by testimony of a specific corruption, like contravening the consensus of scholars, or contradicting the doctrine of the arguer, and the like. The evidence here is what the questioner shows of the specific corruption, such as the consensus of scholars on the nonoccurrence of the implication from the evidence in the case of the refutation, and such as the arguer's evidence not being valid as a proof in his own doctrine. Here are clarifying examples: Examples of the resemblance refutation:  A) The arguer jurist: It is not permissible to hire a worker for wages whose realization and amount are unknown (the claim), because it is a transaction involving gharar, and every such transaction is invalid. (An evidence that, in the arguer's view, produces his claim). The questioner: This evidence is refuted by the consensus of scholars on the non-occurrence of its implication in the company of qirad, for it is a type of transaction for work with wages whose realization is unknown, due to the possibility of profit and loss, and of unknown specific amount, because one-third or one-half is not known how much it will be except after it occurs. Thus the realization of profit is unknown, and the amount of profit is unknown. (A resemblance refutation by which the questioner nullified the arguer's evidence due to the specific corruption, which here is the consensus of scholars on the non-occurrence of its implication in the case of the refutation that he presented, i.e., the qirad company. Thus the evidence is disagreed with by consensus in the case of the refutation). B) The arguer jurist (Shafi'i and Maliki): The marriage of a fornicatress is not prohibited (the claim). Because the saying of Allah Most High: "The fornicatress shall not be married except to a fornicator or idolater, and that is forbidden to the believers" is abrogated by His saying: "And marry the unmarried among you." (An evidence that, in the arguer's view, produces his claim). The questioner: This evidence is refuted, because the abrogator is more general than the abrogated; indeed the term 'al-ayama' includes chaste women, fornicatresses, males and females, and it is not permissible in the arguer's doctrine to abrogate the specific with the general. So it is an invalid evidence according to the arguer's doctrine. (A resemblance refutation by which the questioner nullified the arguer's evidence, due to a specific corruption, which here is the arguer's reliance on what contradicts his own doctrine). (1) It is the company in which the capital is from one of the two partners, and the work is from the other partner, on the condition that the profit is between them; the owner of the capital has a share, and the worker has a share, according to their agreement. As for the loss: the owner of the capital loses from his capital, and the worker loses what he expended of effort.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the 'resemblance refutation' (al-naqd al-shabihi). The author begins by stating that it is the invalidation of a claim by pointing out a specific corruption (fasad makhsus) in the argument, such as contradicting scholarly consensus (ijma') or contradicting the arguer's own doctrinal school (madhhab). The 'witness' (shahid) is the evidence the questioner brings to show that corruption. Two examples are given. In the first, a jurist argues that hiring a worker with unknown wages is invalid due to gharar. The questioner refutes by pointing to the qirad company (mudaraba), where profit/ loss is unknown yet it is valid by consensus. Thus the arguer's evidence fails in that case. In the second, a Shafi'i/Maliki jurist claims that marrying a fornicatress is not forbidden, citing an abrogated verse. The questioner notes that the abrogator (general) is more general than the abrogated (specific), which violates the arguer's own principle that a general cannot abrogate a specific. Both examples show how the refutation highlights a specific flaw in the arguer's evidence or its consistency with his own doctrine.

Key Terms
نقض شبيهي (naqd shabihi): A refutation that invalidates a claim by demonstrating a specific corruption in the evidence, such as contradicting consensus or the arguer's own doctrine. [Note: Stable term in jadal literature; no significant shift.]
فساد مخصوص (fasad makhsus): A specific flaw or corruption in the argument, such as inconsistency with consensus or the arguer's own school.
إجماع (ijma'): Scholarly consensus, used here as a criterion for validity or invalidity of an argument in dialectical refutation. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ijma' is a source of law; here it serves as a standard for argument soundness.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of naqd shabihi, what specific corruption does the questioner use to refute the arguer's claim about hiring with unknown wages?
A) The arguer's evidence is based on a weak hadith.
B) The consensus of scholars on the validity of qirad company contradicts the arguer's claim.
C) The arguer's evidence involves an unlawful transaction.
D) The arguer's claim is opposed by a stronger analogy.

Key Takeaway
A successful naqd shabihi uses a specific corruption—consensus or doctrinal inconsistency—to invalidate the arguer's evidence without directly refuting the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a naqd shabihi against the argument that 'selling grapes is permissible because they are not intoxicating' by pointing to the consensus that khamr (wine) is prohibited from grapes.
?
Analyze the second example: What is the specific corruption in the second example? Why does it invalidate the evidence according to the arguer's own doctrine?

Two Responses to Refutation: Man' and Intiqal
Author explains the arguer's two possible responses to a refutation: denial (man') or shifting to a new proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two primary responses to a refutation in Islamic dialectic (man' and intiqal) and distinguish the sub-types of man'.

Core Idea
In jadal, a refuted arguer has two moves: deny the refutation or switch proofs.

When an arguer's evidence is challenged by a refutation (naqd), he does not simply accept defeat. Islamic dialectic provides two legitimate responses: denial (man') and transition (intiqal). This lesson unpacks each response, focusing on the logic behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main responses
The passage presents two distinct ways an arguer can answer a refutation: man' (denial) and intiqal (transition). Note which is described first.
Man' is further subdivided.
2
Understand denial (man')
Denial involves rejecting that the refutation applies. This can be done by clarifying the intended meaning of the evidence or the claim itself. The passage says you can 'clarify what is intended by the evidence or the claim'.
3
Understand transition (intiqal)
Transition means abandoning the first evidence and providing a new proof for the same claim. The passage says the arguer 'has been refuted concerning his first evidence' and begins anew.
4
Recognize the sub-type of denial: denying complete evidence
The passage elaborates that the arguer can deny that the complete version of his evidence exists in the refutation case. If it were complete, the conclusion would follow; since it doesn't, the refutation fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجوبة المعلّل على نقض السائل:
للمعلّل أن يجيب على نقض السائل بأحد طريقين:
الأول: (المنع) وفق قواعد المنع وضوابطه السابقة، وقد يكون المنع بتحرير المراد من الدليل، أو بتحرير المراد من الدعوى.
الثاني: الانتقال إلى دليل آخر يثبت به مدّعاه، ويكون بهذا الانتقال قد أفحم من جهة دليله الأول، وأخذ في محاولة إثبات مدّعاه بدليل جديد.
شرح جواب المعلّل بالمنع على نقض السائل:
للمعلّل أن يمنع نقض السائل بوجوه:
١ ـ فإما أن يمنع وجود تمام دليل الدعوى في صورة النقض، فيقول للسائل: دليلي غير موجود على التمام فيما أوردته من نقض، ولو كان موجوداً على التمام لما تخلّف مدلوله عنه.
The answers of the arguer to the refutation of the questioner: The arguer can respond to the questioner's refutation in one of two ways: The first: (Denial) according to the rules and conditions of denial that preceded, and the denial may be by clarifying what is intended by the evidence, or by clarifying what is intended by the claim. The second: Moving to another evidence by which he proves his claim, and by this move he has been refuted concerning his first evidence, and begins attempting to prove his claim with a new evidence. Explanation of the arguer's response by denial to the refutation of the questioner: The arguer can deny the refutation of the questioner in ways: 1. Either he can deny the presence of the complete evidence for the claim in the case of the refutation, so he says to the questioner: My evidence is not completely present in what you have presented as a refutation, and if it were completely present, its implication would not be absent from it.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage outlines how the arguer (mu'allil) can respond to a refutation (naqd) from the questioner. Two main methods are given. The first is 'denial' (man'), which follows specific rules: the arguer can clarify the intended meaning of his evidence or his claim, thereby showing that the refutation does not actually apply. The second method is to abandon the first argument and move to a new evidence to prove the same claim. This shift implies that the arguer acknowledges the weakness of the initial proof and attempts a fresh approach. The author then elaborates on the denial method, stating that the arguer can deny that the complete version of his evidence is present in the case cited by the questioner. If it were fully present, the expected conclusion would follow, but since it does not, the refutation is invalid. This is a key technique in dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms
منع
انتقال
تمام الدليل

Key Takeaway
The arguer can either deny the refutation's applicability (man') or shift to a new proof (intiqal), with man' including clarification of evidence or claim.

Two Responses to a Naqd: Deny or Shift
Author introduces two responses to a naqd: denial or shifting proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two main responses (man' and intiqal) a mu'allil uses to answer a naqd (objection) in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
When your proof is refuted, you can either deny the objection or switch to a new proof.

In a jadal debate, when a questioner (sa'il) points out a flaw in your proof (naqd), you as the mu'allil have two strategic options: deny the validity of the objection (man') or abandon that proof and present another (intiqal). This passage from a classical jadal work explains these two choices and sets the stage for the detailed forms of man' that follow. Understanding this fork is essential for any Muslim intellectual who needs to defend positions in theological, legal, or philosophical discussions without falling into logical errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (naqd)
Read the fiqh example at the beginning of the passage. The questioner objects that the mu'allil's proof is invalid because it uses an abrogating verse that is more general than the abrogated verse, which contradicts the mu'allil's own madhhab. This is the naqd.
The objection is called 'naqd shabihi' because it points to a specific corruption (fasad makhsus).
2
Locate the mu'allil's two options
In the Arabic after the objection, find the sentence: 'للمعلّل أن يجيب على نقض السائل بأحد طريقين: الأول: (المنع) ... الثاني: الانتقال إلى دليل آخر'. This clearly states the two responses.
3
Define 'man'' and 'intiqal'
Man' means denial: the mu'allil rejects the validity of the objection based on established rules. Intiqal means transition: the mu'allil admits the weakness of his first proof and moves to a new one to support his claim.
Intiqal implies the first proof is abandoned, but the claim can still be established.
4
Understand when each is appropriate
The passage indicates that man' is used when the mu'allil believes the objection can be rebutted using one of the four forms of denial. Intiqal is used when the first proof is truly weak, but the mu'allil still wants to defend the original claim.
This shows that intiqal is not a defeat; it is a strategic retreat to a stronger position.
5
Apply the distinction to the fiqh example
If the mu'allil chooses man', he might argue that his proof is complete despite the generality issue. If he chooses intiqal, he would present a different proof for the permissibility of marrying a fornicatress, perhaps using a different verse or analogy.
The passage does not show which choice is better; that depends on the strength of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب ـ المعلّل الفقيه الشافعي والمالكي: لا يحرم نكاح الزانية (المدعى). لأن قول الله تعالى: ﴿الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زانٍ أو مشرك وحُرِّم ذلك على المؤمنين﴾ منسوخ بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وأنكحوا الأيامى منكم ﴾. (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدّعاه) السائل: هذا دليل منقوض، لأن الناسخ فيه أعم من المنسوخ، إذ لفظ (الأيامى) يشملُ العفائف والزواني والذكور والإناث، ولا يجوز في مذهب المعلل نسخ الخاص بالعام، فهو دليل باطل بمقتضى مذهب المعلّل. (نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل، بسبب فساد مخصوص، هو هنا اعتماد المعلّل على ما يخالف مذهبه). أجوبة المعلّل على نقض السائل: للمعلّل أن يجيب على نقض السائل بأحد طريقين: الأول: (المنع) وفق قواعد المنع وضوابطه السابقة، وقد يكون المنع بتحرير المراد من الدليل، أو بتحرير المراد من الدعوى. الثاني: الانتقال إلى دليل آخر يثبت به مدّعاه، ويكون بهذا الانتقال قد أفحم من جهة دليله الأول، وأخذ في محاولة إثبات مدّعاه بدليل جديد. شرح جواب المعلّل بالمنع على نقض السائل: للمعلّل أن يمنع نقض السائل بوجوه: ١ ـ فإما أن يمنع وجود تمام دليل الدعوى في صورة النقض، فيقول للسائل: دليلي غير موجود على التمام فيما أوردته من نقض، ولو كان موجوداً على التمام لما تخلّف مدلوله عنه. ومن أمثلته ما سبق في أمثلة النقض المكسور. هذا إذا كان الواقع كذلك . وقد يلجأ هنا إلى تحرير مراده من الدليل، ويبين بذلك عدم تخلف المدلول عنه . ٢ - وإما أن يمنع تخلف المدلول عن الدليل في صورة النقض، إذا رأى أن الأمر كذلك، فيقول للسائل : المدلول موجود غير متخلف . وقد يلجأ هنا إلى تحرير مراده من الدعوى، ويبين بذلك عدم تخلف المدلول عن الدليل . ٣ - وإما أن يمنع كون الدليل يستلزم المحال، فيبين أن هذا الاستلزام غير وارد في الدليل . وقد يلجأ هنا إلى تحرير مراده من الدليل، ويبين بذلك عدم استلزامه للمحال . ٤ - وإما أن يمنع وجود الاستحالة فيما رآه السائل محالاً، كأن يكون الدور من قبيل الدور المَعِيّ، لا السبقي . أمثلة : أ - المعلل : هذا الشكل الهندسي مربع (مدعى). لأنه سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية، وكل سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية فهو مربع . (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلل مدعاه) السائل : هذا الدليل منقوض، لأنه يجري على مدعى آخر هو المعين، فهو أيضاً سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية، وليس بمربع لأنه لا يشتمل على أربع زوايا قائمة .
B—The mu‘allil, the Shafi‘i and Maliki jurist: The marriage of a fornicatress is not forbidden (the claim). Because the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'The fornicatress—none marries her except a fornicator or an idolater, and that is forbidden to the believers' is abrogated by His saying: 'And marry the unmarried among you.' (A proof that produced, in the mu‘allil's view, his claim.) The questioner: This proof is refuted (manqud), because the abrogating [verse] is more general than the abrogated [verse], since the word 'the unmarried' includes chaste women, fornicatresses, males, and females, and it is not permissible in the mu‘allil's school to abrogate the specific by the general; so it is an invalid proof according to the mu‘allil's school. (A resemblance objection by which the questioner invalidated the mu‘allil's proof, due to a specific corruption, which here is the mu‘allil's reliance on what contradicts his school.) The mu‘allil's answers to the questioner's objection: The mu‘allil can answer the questioner's objection in one of two ways: First: Denial (al-man‘) according to the previous rules and conditions of denial, and denial may be by clarifying the intended meaning of the proof, or by clarifying the intended meaning of the claim. Second: Moving to another proof by which he establishes his claim, and by this move he has been refuted regarding his first proof and begins attempting to establish his claim with a new proof. Explanation of the mu‘allil's answer by denial to the questioner's objection: The mu‘allil may deny the questioner's objection in ways: 1—Either he denies the existence of the complete proof of the claim in the form of the objection, saying to the questioner: My proof does not exist in its entirety in what you have brought as an objection; and if it existed in its entirety, its implication would not be absent. Among its examples is what preceded in the examples of broken objection. This if the reality is such. And he may resort here to clarifying his intended meaning of the proof, thereby showing the non-absence of its implication. 2—Or he denies the absence of the implication from the proof in the form of the objection, if he sees that the matter is such, saying to the questioner: The implication exists, not absent. And he may resort here to clarifying his intended meaning of the claim, thereby showing the non-absence of the implication from the proof. 3—Or he denies that the proof entails an impossibility (muhal), showing that this entailment does not occur in the proof. And he may resort here to clarifying his intended meaning of the proof, thereby showing its non-entailment of the impossibility. 4—Or he denies the existence of impossibility in what the questioner considered impossible, such as circularity being of the type of ma‘i circularity, not sabqi. Examples: A—The mu‘allil: This geometric shape is a square (claim). Because it is a surface bounded by four equal lines, and every surface bounded by four equal lines is a square. (A proof that produced, in the mu‘allil's view, his claim.) The questioner: This proof is refuted, because it applies to another claim, namely the rhombus; it too is a surface bounded by four equal lines, but it is not a square because it does not contain four right angles.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition, drawn from the art of jadal (debate), explaining how a mu‘allil (proponent of a claim) can respond when his proof is subjected to a naqd (refutation) by the sa’il (questioner). The passage opens with a concrete example from fiqh: a Shafi‘i or Maliki jurist argues that marriage to a fornicatress is not forbidden (claim), supporting it with the claim that the verse prohibiting such marriage (Qur’an 24:3) is abrogated by the general command to marry the unmarried (Qur’an 24:32). The questioner objects (naqd) by pointing out that the abrogating verse is more general (a‘amm) than the abrogated verse (khass), and within the mu‘allil's own madhhab (Shafi‘i or Maliki), abrogation of the specific by the general is not permitted. Therefore, the proof is invalid by the mu‘allil's own principles. This is labeled a 'resemblance objection' (naqd shabihi) because the flaw is a specific corruption (fasad makhsus)—here, the mu‘allil's reliance on a principle contrary to his school. The passage then explains that the mu‘allil has two primary ways to answer such a naqd: first, by man‘ (denial) according to established rules, which may involve clarifying the intended meaning (tahrir al-murad) of either the proof or the claim; second, by intiqal (transition) to another proof, thereby conceding the weakness of the first proof but attempting to establish the claim anew. The author then elaborates on the first method (man‘), listing four distinct ways it can be executed: (1) Denying that the complete proof exists in the objection scenario—i.e., the proof as stated is not fully present in the counterexample; (2) Denying that the implication (madlul) fails to follow from the proof in the objection scenario—i.e., the claim is still entailed; (3) Denying that the proof entails an impossibility (muhal)—i.e., the entailment alleged by the questioner does not hold; (4) Denying the existence of the impossibility itself, such as when the questioner claims circularity (dawr), but the circularity is of the type al-mu‘i (simultaneous) rather than al-sabqi (precedential), which may be acceptable. A second example from geometry illustrates: a mu‘allil claims a shape is a square because it is a surface bounded by four equal lines, but the questioner objects that the same definition applies to a rhombus, which is not a square (lacking right angles). This objection would be answered by one of the four forms of man‘—for instance, by denying that the implication (that it is a square) fails, or by clarifying that the proof requires additional conditions (like right angles). The passage thus provides a systematic toolkit for dialectical defense, emphasizing that the mu‘allil is not helpless but can strategically refine or shift his argument.

Key Terms
نقض
المنع
الانتقال
تحرير المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two main ways a mu‘allil can respond to a naqd (objection)?
A) Denial (man‘) and acceptance (qabul)
B) Denial (man‘) and contradiction (tanāquḍ)
C) Denial (man‘) and transition (intiqal)
D) Transition (intiqal) and surrender (istislām)

Key Takeaway
A mu‘allil faced with a naqd can either deny the objection (man‘) or transition to a new proof (intiqal); these are the two principled responses in jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether qiyas (analogical reasoning) can establish a ruling in the presence of a seemingly contradictory text, you are the mu‘allil defending the use of qiyas. The questioner brings a naqd: 'Your qiyas is invalid because the text explicitly states the opposite.' The Four Forms of Man' (Denial)
Author details four forms of denial (man‘) as responses to a naqd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four distinct forms of man' (denial) that a mu'allil can use to respond to a naqd, and explain the specific focus of each form.

Core Idea
Denial (man') has four targets: the proof's completeness, the implication, the entailment of impossibility, or the impossibility itself.

When a mu'allil chooses to deny the objection (man‘), he doesn't simply say 'no'. The passage enumerates four specific ways to execute this denial, each addressing a different aspect of the objection. Understanding these forms allows you to craft precise rebuttals and recognize when an opponent's denial is sound. This granularity is a hallmark of the Islamic jadal tradition.

Guided Path
1
List the four forms of man'
From the Arabic, identify the four numbered ways: (1) denying the completeness of the proof in the objection, (2) denying the absence of the implication, (3) denying that the proof entails impossibility, (4) denying the impossibility itself. Write them down.
The author uses 'إما أن يمنع' for each.
2
Understand each form's core claim
For form 1, the mu‘allil says the proof is not fully present. For form 2, he says the implication does follow. For form 3, he says the proof does not lead to impossibility. For form 4, he says what the questioner thinks is impossible is actually possible.
Each form targets a different component of the objection.
3
Note the use of 'tahrir al-murad'
The passage mentions that clarifying the intended meaning (tahrir al-murad) of the proof or the claim can support the denial. This is a key technique in jadal to refine arguments.
Tahrir al-murad appears in forms 1, 2, and 3.
4
Recognize the common thread
All four forms aim to show that the objection does not invalidate the proof. They are not mutually exclusive; the mu‘allil may use one or combine them.
The choice depends on the nature of the objection.
5
Apply to the fiqh example
In the fiqh example, the mu‘allil could use form 1 by denying that his proof is incomplete (arguing that the abrogation is valid). Or form 2 by denying that the implication fails (claiming the conclusion still follows). Which form might be most effective?

ب ـ المعلّل الفقيه الشافعي والمالكي: لا يحرم نكاح الزانية (المدعى). لأن قول الله تعالى: ﴿الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زانٍ أو مشرك وحُرِّم ذلك على المؤمنين﴾ منسوخ بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وأنكحوا الأيامى منكم ﴾. (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلّل مدّعاه) السائل: هذا دليل منقوض، لأن الناسخ فيه أعم من المنسوخ، إذ لفظ (الأيامى) يشملُ العفائف والزواني والذكور والإناث، ولا يجوز في مذهب المعلل نسخ الخاص بالعام، فهو دليل باطل بمقتضى مذهب المعلّل. (نقض شبيهي أبطل به السائل دليل المعلّل، بسبب فساد مخصوص، هو هنا اعتماد المعلّل على ما يخالف مذهبه). أجوبة المعلّل على نقض السائل: للمعلّل أن يجيب على نقض السائل بأحد طريقين: الأول: (المنع) وفق قواعد المنع وضوابطه السابقة، وقد يكون المنع بتحرير المراد من الدليل، أو بتحرير المراد من الدعوى. الثاني: الانتقال إلى دليل آخر يثبت به مدّعاه، ويكون بهذا الانتقال قد أفحم من جهة دليله الأول، وأخذ في محاولة إثبات مدّعاه بدليل جديد. شرح جواب المعلّل بالمنع على نقض السائل: للمعلّل أن يمنع نقض السائل بوجوه: ١ ـ فإما أن يمنع وجود تمام دليل الدعوى في صورة النقض، فيقول للسائل: دليلي غير موجود على التمام فيما أوردته من نقض، ولو كان موجوداً على التمام لما تخلّف مدلوله عنه. ومن أمثلته ما سبق في أمثلة النقض المكسور. هذا إذا كان الواقع كذلك . وقد يلجأ هنا إلى تحرير مراده من الدليل، ويبين بذلك عدم تخلف المدلول عنه . ٢ - وإما أن يمنع تخلف المدلول عن الدليل في صورة النقض، إذا رأى أن الأمر كذلك، فيقول للسائل : المدلول موجود غير متخلف . وقد يلجأ هنا إلى تحرير مراده من الدعوى، ويبين بذلك عدم تخلف المدلول عن الدليل . ٣ - وإما أن يمنع كون الدليل يستلزم المحال، فيبين أن هذا الاستلزام غير وارد في الدليل . وقد يلجأ هنا إلى تحرير مراده من الدليل، ويبين بذلك عدم استلزامه للمحال . ٤ - وإما أن يمنع وجود الاستحالة فيما رآه السائل محالاً، كأن يكون الدور من قبيل الدور المَعِيّ، لا السبقي . أمثلة : أ - المعلل : هذا الشكل الهندسي مربع (مدعى). لأنه سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية، وكل سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية فهو مربع . (دليل أنتج في نظر المعلل مدعاه) السائل : هذا الدليل منقوض، لأنه يجري على مدعى آخر هو المعين، فهو أيضاً سطح يحيط به أربعة خطوط متساوية، وليس بمربع لأنه لا يشتمل على أربع زوايا قائمة .
B—The mu‘allil, the Shafi‘i and Maliki jurist: The marriage of a fornicatress is not forbidden (the claim). Because the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'The fornicatress—none marries her except a fornicator or an idolater, and that is forbidden to the believers' is abrogated by His saying: 'And marry the unmarried among you.' (A proof that produced, in the mu‘allil's view, his claim.) The questioner: This proof is refuted (manqud), because the abrogating [verse] is more general than the abrogated [verse], since the word 'the unmarried' includes chaste women, fornicatresses, males, and females, and it is not permissible in the mu‘allil's school to abrogate the specific by the general; so it is an invalid proof according to the mu‘allil's school. (A resemblance objection by which the questioner invalidated the mu‘allil's proof, due to a specific corruption, which here is the mu‘allil's reliance on what contradicts his school.) The mu‘allil's answers to the questioner's objection: The mu‘allil can answer the questioner's objection in one of two ways: First: Denial (al-man‘) according to the previous rules and conditions of denial, and denial may be by clarifying the intended meaning of the proof, or by clarifying the intended meaning of the claim. Second: Moving to another proof by which he establishes his claim, and by this move he has been refuted regarding his first proof and begins attempting to establish his claim with a new proof. Explanation of the mu‘allil's answer by denial to the questioner's objection: The mu‘allil may deny the questioner's objection in ways: 1—Either he denies the existence of the complete proof of the claim in the form of the objection, saying to the questioner: My proof does not exist in its entirety in what you have brought as an objection; and if it existed in its entirety, its implication would not be absent. Among its examples is what preceded in the examples of broken objection. This if the reality is such. And he may resort here to clarifying his intended meaning of the proof, thereby showing the non-absence of its implication. 2—Or he denies the absence of the implication from the proof in the form of the objection, if he sees that the matter is such, saying to the questioner: The implication exists, not absent. And he may resort here to clarifying his intended meaning of the claim, thereby showing the non-absence of the implication from the proof. 3—Or he denies that the proof entails an impossibility (muhal), showing that this entailment does not occur in the proof. And he may resort here to clarifying his intended meaning of the proof, thereby showing its non-entailment of the impossibility. 4—Or he denies the existence of impossibility in what the questioner considered impossible, such as circularity being of the type of ma‘i circularity, not sabqi. Examples: A—The mu‘allil: This geometric shape is a square (claim). Because it is a surface bounded by four equal lines, and every surface bounded by four equal lines is a square. (A proof that produced, in the mu‘allil's view, his claim.) The questioner: This proof is refuted, because it applies to another claim, namely the rhombus; it too is a surface bounded by four equal lines, but it is not a square because it does not contain four right angles.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition, drawn from the art of jadal (debate), explaining how a mu‘allil (proponent of a claim) can respond when his proof is subjected to a naqd (refutation) by the sa’il (questioner). The passage opens with a concrete example from fiqh: a Shafi‘i or Maliki jurist argues that marriage to a fornicatress is not forbidden (claim), supporting it with the claim that the verse prohibiting such marriage (Qur’an 24:3) is abrogated by the general command to marry the unmarried (Qur’an 24:32). The questioner objects (naqd) by pointing out that the abrogating verse is more general (a‘amm) than the abrogated verse (khass), and within the mu‘allil's own madhhab (Shafi‘i or Maliki), abrogation of the specific by the general is not permitted. Therefore, the proof is invalid by the mu‘allil's own principles. This is labeled a 'resemblance objection' (naqd shabihi) because the flaw is a specific corruption (fasad makhsus)—here, the mu‘allil's reliance on a principle contrary to his school. The passage then explains that the mu‘allil has two primary ways to answer such a naqd: first, by man‘ (denial) according to established rules, which may involve clarifying the intended meaning (tahrir al-murad) of either the proof or the claim; second, by intiqal (transition) to another proof, thereby conceding the weakness of the first proof but attempting to establish the claim anew. The author then elaborates on the first method (man‘), listing four distinct ways it can be executed: (1) Denying that the complete proof exists in the objection scenario—i.e., the proof as stated is not fully present in the counterexample; (2) Denying that the implication (madlul) fails to follow from the proof in the objection scenario—i.e., the claim is still entailed; (3) Denying that the proof entails an impossibility (muhal)—i.e., the entailment alleged by the questioner does not hold; (4) Denying the existence of the impossibility itself, such as when the questioner claims circularity (dawr), but the circularity is of the type al-mu‘i (simultaneous) rather than al-sabqi (precedential), which may be acceptable. A second example from geometry illustrates: a mu‘allil claims a shape is a square because it is a surface bounded by four equal lines, but the questioner objects that the same definition applies to a rhombus, which is not a square (lacking right angles). This objection would be answered by one of the four forms of man‘—for instance, by denying that the implication (that it is a square) fails, or by clarifying that the proof requires additional conditions (like right angles). The passage thus provides a systematic toolkit for dialectical defense, emphasizing that the mu‘allil is not helpless but can strategically refine or shift his argument.

المنع
المدلول
المحال
الدور المَعِيّ
تحرير المراد

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which form of man‘ involves the mu‘allil arguing that what the questioner considers an impossibility (like circularity) is actually not impossible?
A) Denying the completeness of the proof
B) Denying the absence of the implication
C) Denying that the proof entails impossibility
D) Denying the existence of impossibility

Key Takeaway
The four forms of man‘ (denial) target the proof's completeness, the implication's presence, the entailment of impossibility, or the impossibility itself, allowing a mu‘allil to respond precisely to a naqd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether the world is created, the questioner objects that the argument for creation implies an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Which of the four forms of man‘ might the mu‘allil use to respond? Explain your choice.
?
The geometry example: the mu‘allil says the shape is a square. The questioner objects that the proof applies to a rhombus. Which form of man‘ would be most natural for the mu‘allil to use? Write a short response in the style of the mu‘allil.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
12 min
Applying Man' with Examples
Author provides examples to illustrate the four forms of man‘.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the fiqh and geometry examples from the passage and determine which form of man‘ is best suited to answer each objection.

Core Idea
Examples from fiqh and geometry show how the four forms of man‘ work in real dialectical situations.

The passage does not just theorize; it provides two illustrative examples: one from Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) and one from geometry. These examples help you see how the abstract forms of man‘ translate into actual debate moves. By studying them, you will sharpen your ability to spot which form of denial fits a given objection.

Guided Path
1
Analyse the fiqh example's objection
The questioner objects that the mu‘allil's proof is invalid because it violates his own madhhab's rule. Identify the core of the objection: the abrogating verse is more general than the abrogated verse.
This is a naqd shabihi (resemblance objection) because the flaw is specific to the mu‘allil's school.
2
Consider possible man' forms for the fiqh example
Could the mu‘allil use form 1 (deny completeness) by arguing that his proof actually includes a nuance that the questioner missed? Or form 2 (deny absence of implication) by showing that despite the generality, the implication still holds? Which seems most plausible?
The mu‘allil might also use form 3 or 4 if the objection leads to a claim of impossibility.
3
Analyse the geometry example's objection
The mu‘allil claims the shape is a square using the definition 'surface bounded by four equal lines'. The questioner objects that a rhombus also fits that definition but is not a square. Identify the flaw: the definition is incomplete (lacks right angles).
This is a classic case of an incomplete definition.
4
Determine the best man' form for the geometry example
The mu‘allil's most natural response would be form 1: deny the completeness of the proof. He could argue that his definition of square implicitly includes right angles (tahrir al-murad), so the proof is complete. Alternatively, form 2 could be used if he insists the implication (that it's a square) still holds despite the rhombus counterexample, but that would be weaker.
Form 3 or 4 are less relevant here because there is no claim of impossibility.
5
Compare the two examples
The fiqh example involves a rule-based objection (madhhab principle), while the geometry example involves a factual/definitional objection. Different forms of man‘ may be more appropriate depending on the type of flaw.
This shows the flexibility of the man' toolkit.
